Ibn Sina's Definition of Jadl
Ibn Sina defines jadl as a skill using widely accepted premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of jadl as a dialectical skill that uses widely accepted premises to constrain an opponent while preserving one's own consistency.

Core Idea
Jadl is the art of using common knowledge to corner an opponent without contradicting yourself.

Ibn Sina, a towering figure in Islamic philosophy, defined jadl (dialectical debate) with precision. This passage captures the essence of his definition, focusing on the type of premises used and the dual aim of constraining the opponent and protecting one's own position. It sets the stage for understanding how dialectic differs from demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read Ibn Sina's definition carefully. Notice the key terms: ṣinā'a (skill), muqaddimāt dhā'i'a (widely accepted premises), and ḥajr (constraint).
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to the structure.
2
Identify the two parts of the definition
The definition has two aims: (1) to constrain the opponent using widely accepted premises, and (2) when answering, to avoid contradicting your own position. See how these are linked.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ibn Sina is not advocating trickery; he is describing a rational method that relies on shared assumptions. Consider how this differs from pure logic or sophistry.
This distinction is important for understanding the ethical dimension of jadl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول ابن سينا في تعريف الجدل : فغرضنا الآن في هذا الفن تحصيل صناعة يمكننا بها أن نأتي بالحجر على كل ما يوضع مطلوباً من مقدمات ذائعة وأن نكون إذا أجبناه يؤخذ منا ما يناقض وضعنا
And Ibn Sina says in the definition of jadl: 'Our purpose now in this art is to acquire a skill by which we can bring constraint upon every matter placed as a problem from premises that are widely accepted, and that when we answer it, there is taken from us what contradicts our position.'
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's definition of jadl as a skill or art (ṣinā'a) that enables one to compel an opponent using widely accepted premises (muqaddimāt dhā'i'a), and when answering, to avoid contradicting one's own position.

Key Terms
صناعة (ṣinā'a)
مقدمات ذائعة (muqaddimāt dhā'i'a)
حجر (ḥajr, constraint)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Sina's definition of jadl permits winning by any means, including sophistry.
Correction: Ibn Sina specifies that the premises must be 'widely accepted' (dhā'i'a), not merely persuasive or false. Jadl is a rational discipline based on shared assumptions, not trickery.

Checkpoint
What type of premises does Ibn Sina say are used in jadl?
A) Certain premises
B) Widely accepted premises
C) Sensory premises
D) Axiomatic premises

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina defines jadl as a skill to constrain opponents using widely accepted premises while avoiding self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina describes jadl as a skill to bring constraint using premises the other person accepts. When you argue for your faith or beliefs, do you take the time to find common ground with your listener, or do you assume they share your starting points? Will you deliberately identify shared premises before your next challenging conversation?
?
The definition also emphasizes avoiding self-contradiction. Recall the last debate or heated discussion you had — did you say anything that contradicted your own stated position? How would preparing a consistent argument beforehand change the outcome? Are you willing to write down your position and test it for contradictions?
?
This passage defines jadl as a skill — something acquired and honed. If you were criticized for being inconsistent in your arguments, would you feel defensive or motivated to improve? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your ability to argue coherently using shared premises?

Aristotle's Definition of Jadl and Comparison with Ibn Sina
Aristotle's definition of jadl with three elements and comparison to Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's definition of jadl, its three elements, and how it differs from Ibn Sina's definition.

Core Idea
Aristotle sees jadl as a method; Ibn Sina sees it as a skill.

This passage introduces Aristotle's definition of jadl (dialectic) from his Topics. The author then compares it with Ibn Sina's definition, highlighting a key difference in how they view the nature of jadl. Understanding this comparison clarifies the place of jadl in logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three elements Aristotle lists.
2
Identify the three elements
Aristotle's definition contains three elements: method, reasoning from widely accepted premises, and avoiding contradiction. Find each in the text.
3
Compare with Ibn Sina
See where Aristotle and Ibn Sina agree (last two elements) and disagree (first element: method vs. skill).
This difference has consequences for the status of jadl in logic.
4
Consider the consequence
For Aristotle, jadl is part of general logic, a weaker form of demonstration. For Ibn Sina, it is a distinct art.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحسن أن نضع إلى جانب هذا التعريف لابن سينا تعريف أرسطو لنبين الفرق بينهما . يقول أرسطو في استهلال الطوبيقا : إن قصدنا في هذا الكتاب أن نستبين طريقاً يتهيأ لنا به أن نعمل من مقدمات ذائعة قياساً في كل مسألة تقصي وأن نكون - إذا أوجدنا جواباً - لم نأت فيه بشيء مضاد يتألف التعريف من عناصر ثلاثة هي : ( أ ) الطريق . (ب) الاستدلال من المشهورات. (جـ) عدم التناقض عند الجواب . ويتفق أرسطو وابن سينا في العنصرين الأخيرين، ويختلفان في العنصر الأول. فالجدل عند أرسطو : طريق أو منهج ، وعند ابن سينا صناعة هذا الفرق له أثره فى اعتبار الجدل ملحقاً بالمنطق العام لأرسطو، أو هو نوع من القياس، ولكنه ليس يقيناً، أي ان المنطق واحد، وطريقه واحد، أعلاه البرهان. والجدل مرتبة أضعف منه، وأما في اعتبار الجدل نوعاً متميزاً من المنطق وصناعة خاصة هي الصناعة الجدلية
And it is good to place alongside this definition of Ibn Sina the definition of Aristotle to show the difference between them. Aristotle says in the introduction of the Topics: 'Our intention in this book is to find a method by which we can make a syllogism from widely accepted premises in every problem we examine, and that when we give an answer, we do not bring anything contradictory.' The definition consists of three elements: (a) the method, (b) reasoning from generally accepted opinions, (c) non-contradiction in answering. Aristotle and Ibn Sina agree on the last two elements but differ on the first. For Aristotle, jadl is a method or approach, while for Ibn Sina it is a skill. This difference affects considering jadl as attached to general logic for Aristotle, or as a type of syllogism but not certain, meaning logic is one and its method is one, the highest of which is demonstration (burhān). And jadl is a weaker level than it. But in the view that jadl is a distinct type of logic and a specific skill, it is the dialectical skill.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's definition of jadl as a method (ṭarīq) using widely accepted premises (muqaddimāt dhā'i'a) to form a syllogism (qiyās) in any inquiry, while ensuring no contradiction in the answer. The author then extracts three elements: method, reasoning from common opinions, and avoidance of contradiction. He compares Aristotle and Ibn Sina: they agree on the latter two but differ on the first – Aristotle sees jadl as a method, Ibn Sina as a skill (ṣinā'a). The consequence is that for Aristotle, jadl is part of general logic, a weaker form of demonstration; for Ibn Sina, it is a distinct logical art.

Key Terms
طريق (ṭarīq)
مشهورات (mashhūrāt)
قياس (qiyās)
برهان (burhān)
صناعة (ṣinā'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that jadl is a free argument without constraints.
Correction: Both Aristotle and Ibn Sina require jadl to be based on widely accepted premises and free of contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the first of the three elements in Aristotle's definition of jadl?
A) The method
B) Widely accepted premises
C) Avoiding contradiction
D) The skill

Key Takeaway
Aristotle defines jadl as a method using widely accepted premises to form a syllogism without contradiction, while Ibn Sina defines it as a distinct skill.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in a debate or discussion, do you approach it as a method to follow (like Aristotle) or as a skill to master (like Ibn Sina)? What does your answer reveal about your priorities in seeking truth?
?
How often do you check your own arguments for contradiction? Are you willing to change your position when you find inconsistency, or do you defend it regardless? What would it cost you to be vulnerable enough to admit error?
?
This lesson shows that jadl uses widely accepted opinions rather than certain truths. In your daily conversations, do you base your arguments on what is commonly accepted, or do you challenge assumptions? If you never challenge, what are you afraid of losing? If you always challenge, are you seeking truth or just winning?

Firuzabadi's Definition of Jadl and Qur'anic Examples
Firuzabadi defines jadl and illustrates it with three Qur'anic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Firuzabadi's definition of jadl and the three types of jadl mentioned in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Jadl means twisting words in dispute; the Qur'an shows it in different forms.

Al-Firuzabadi defines jadl as a form of opposition through dispute, rooted in the physical act of twisting. He then illustrates this with three Qur'anic examples, showing that jadl can occur between prophets and their people, between aggressors and truth, and even between a prophet and angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice how al-Firuzabadi introduces his definition and then lists examples.
2
Understand the definition and etymology
Focus on the definition: 'jadl is opposition in the manner of dispute and contention.' Note the etymological link to twisting a rope firmly, which suggests a mutual attempt to sway the opponent.
3
Identify the three Qur'anic examples
The passage enumerates three instances of jadl in the Qur'an: Noah with his people, disputers against prophets, and Abraham with angels. Consider the different contexts and parties involved.
Notice that jadl can be used by prophets or by wrongdoers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أردنا أن نتعرف على رأي مجد الدين محمد بن يعقوب الفيروز بادي . في تعريف الجدل فإنه يقول : الجدل: هو المعارضة على سبيل المنازعة والمغالبة، وأصله من جدل الشيء أحكم فتله ، كأن كلا من المتجادلين يقتل الآخر عن رأيه . وقد ورد الجدل في القرآن على وجوه مختلفة . الأول: معارضة نوح وقومه . قال تعالى : يا نُوحُ قَدْ جَادِلْتَنَا فَأَكْثَرْتَ جِدَالَنَا) . الثاني: مجادلة أهل العدوان . قال تعالى : (أَتُجَادِلُونَني في أَسْمَاءٍ سَمَّيْتُمُوهَا) . الثالث : جدال إبراهيم والملائكة في باب قوم لوط . قال تعالى : (يُجَادِلُنَا في قَوْمِ لُوطٍ)
And if we want to know the opinion of Majd al-Din Muhammad ibn Ya‘qub al-Firuzabadi on the definition of jadl, he says: 'Jadl is: opposition in the manner of dispute and contention, and its origin is from 'jadl' of a thing, meaning to twist it firmly, as if each of the two disputants twists the other from his opinion.' And jadl has occurred in the Qur'an in different ways. First: the opposition of Noah and his people. He said (the Almighty): 'O Noah, you have disputed with us and disputed much.' Second: the dispute of the people of aggression. He said (the Almighty): 'Do you dispute with me concerning names you have named?' Third: the dispute of Abraham with the angels concerning the people of Lot. He said (the Almighty): 'He disputes with us concerning the people of Lot.'
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage gives al-Firuzabadi's definition of jadl as opposition (mu‘āraḍa) in the manner of dispute and contention, and he traces its origin to the physical twisting of a rope. Then he lists three types of jadl in the Qur'an: the dispute of Noah with his people, the dispute of the opponents of truth with their prophets, and the dispute of Abraham with the angels about Lot's people.

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-mu‘āraḍa)
فتل (fatl, twisting)
جِدَال (jidāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jadl in the Qur'an is always negative and blameworthy.
Correction: While some jadl is negative, the Qur'an also presents jadl by prophets (Noah and Abraham) and even by angels, showing it can be a legitimate form of argument in certain contexts.

Checkpoint
According to al-Firuzabadi, what is the origin of the word 'jadl'?
A) From the word for earth
B) From the word for twisting firmly
C) From the word for argument
D) From the word for victory

Key Takeaway
Al-Firuzabadi defines jadl as opposition through dispute, rooted in twisting, and the Qur'an presents three distinct contexts for it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own arguments—whether with family, friends, or online—do you find yourself 'twisting' their words to gain an upper hand? How does al-Firuzabadi's definition of jadl as 'twisting the other from his opinion' expose a motive you might have been hiding from yourself?
?
The Qur'an shows prophets like Noah and Abraham engaged in jadl for the sake of truth. When you argue, is your goal to defend truth or to defend your ego? Be honest: in your last disagreement, were you more concerned with being 'right' or with bringing the other person closer to what is true?
?
Al-Firuzabadi says jadl is 'opposition in the manner of dispute and contention.' Think of a time you opposed someone. Did you do so contending or cooperating? What would it take for you to transform your disputes into respectful dialogue that seeks truth rather than victory?

Tawhid of the Creator: There is No God but Allah
Introduction to the first aspect of tawhid: oneness of Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of tawhid: affirming the oneness of the Creator as the foundation of Islamic belief.

Core Idea
Tawhid begins with declaring there is no god but Allah alone.

Islam’s call to tawhid is not a single concept but a threefold message. The first and most fundamental aspect is the oneness of the Creator: the declaration that no deity is worthy of worship except Allah. This lesson unpacks that core assertion from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening phrase
Read the Arabic sentence 'ثم جاء الإسلام، جاء الإسلام بالدعوة إلى التوحيد، توحيد الخالق فلا إله إلا الله' and its translation. Notice how the text emphasizes the arrival of Islam as a call to tawhid.
2
Identify the first aspect
Focus on 'توحيد الخالق' (tawhid of the Creator). Understand that this means affirming that Allah is the sole Creator and the only being worthy of worship.
3
Reflect on the declaration
The phrase 'فلا إله إلا الله' sums up this aspect. Ask yourself why this declaration is the foundation of Islam.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جاء الإسلام، جاء الإسلام بالدعوة إلى التوحيد، توحيد الخالق فلا إله إلا الله. وتوحيد العقيدة فلا دين إلا الإسلام، وتوحيد البشرية : كلكم لأدم وآدم من تراب.
Then Islam came, Islam came with the call to tawhid (monotheism): the oneness of the Creator, so there is no god but Allah; and the oneness of creed, so there is no religion but Islam; and the oneness of humanity: 'All of you are from Adam, and Adam is from dust.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage states the core message of Islam as a call to tawhid in three distinct aspects. First, tawhid of the Creator (tawḥīd al-khāliq): affirming that there is no deity but Allah alone. Second, tawhid of creed (tawḥīd al-ʿaqīda): that the only accepted religion before Allah is Islam. Third, tawhid of humanity (tawḥīd al-bashariyya): all human beings are equal in origin, descending from Adam who was created from dust. The phrase 'kullukum li-Adam wa Adam min turāb' is a hadith emphasizing human equality. This threefold division highlights that tawhid encompasses theology, religious law, and social unity.

Key Terms
التوحيد (al-tawḥīd)
توحيد الخالق (tawḥīd al-khāliq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of tawhid mentioned in this passage?
A) Oneness of creed
B) Oneness of humanity
C) Oneness of the Creator
D) Oneness of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The first aspect of tawhid is affirming the oneness of the Creator through the declaration 'La ilaha illa Allah'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you truly made 'لا إله إلا الله' the ruling principle of your life, or have you allowed it to become a mere slogan while your heart is attached to other 'gods' like status, wealth, or approval? What is one attachment you need to break this week to live out this declaration?
?
If the very first thing Islam called to is the oneness of the Creator, how much time and effort do you spend reinforcing this foundation in your daily life? Are you more concerned with secondary issues than with the core of your faith? What can you do today to recalibrate your priorities?
?
You read that the declaration 'لا إله إلا الله' is the direct consequence of knowing Allah as the Creator. Does your heart truly feel that all else is nothing before Him? When you face a difficult situation, do you instinctively turn to Him alone, or do you rely on people, habits, or your own strength? Commit now to one specific moment tomorrow where you will consciously remember 'لا إله إلا الله' and act on it.

Tawhid of Humanity: All from Adam, Adam from Dust
Third aspect of tawhid: oneness of humanity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third aspect of tawhid: the oneness of humanity, which establishes human equality and brotherhood.

Core Idea
Tawhid unites all humans as equal descendants of Adam.

The third aspect of tawhid extends beyond theology and religion to embrace all humanity. It declares that all human beings are equal, descending from Adam who was created from dust. This lesson unpacks that final dimension from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the phrase on humanity
Read 'وتوحيد البشرية: كلكم لآدم وآدم من تراب' and its translation. Note the use of a hadith to support the point.
2
Understand 'tawhid of humanity'
Tawhid of humanity (توحيد البشرية) means recognizing the common origin of all people—from Adam, who was created from dust—and thus their fundamental equality.
3
Reflect on social implications
Consider how this teaching opposes racism, tribalism, and class divisions. How does tawhid foster unity among diverse peoples?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جاء الإسلام، جاء الإسلام بالدعوة إلى التوحيد، توحيد الخالق فلا إله إلا الله. وتوحيد العقيدة فلا دين إلا الإسلام، وتوحيد البشرية : كلكم لأدم وآدم من تراب.
Then Islam came, Islam came with the call to tawhid (monotheism): the oneness of the Creator, so there is no god but Allah; and the oneness of creed, so there is no religion but Islam; and the oneness of humanity: 'All of you are from Adam, and Adam is from dust.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage states the core message of Islam as a call to tawhid in three distinct aspects. First, tawhid of the Creator (tawḥīd al-khāliq): affirming that there is no deity but Allah alone. Second, tawhid of creed (tawḥīd al-ʿaqīda): that the only accepted religion before Allah is Islam. Third, tawhid of humanity (tawḥīd al-bashariyya): all human beings are equal in origin, descending from Adam who was created from dust. The phrase 'kullukum li-Adam wa Adam min turāb' is a hadith emphasizing human equality. This threefold division highlights that tawhid encompasses theology, religious law, and social unity.

Key Terms
توحيد البشرية (tawḥīd al-bashariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the oneness of humanity emphasize?
A) That all humans should follow Islam
B) That all humans are equal beings from Adam
C) That humans are superior to other creatures
D) That humans can become gods

Key Takeaway
Tawhid of humanity teaches that all people are equal, sharing a common origin from Adam, created from dust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat people of different races, nationalities, or backgrounds as genuinely equal, or do you subconsciously consider yourself superior? In light of the hadith that all are from Adam, what specific step will you take this week to practice true equality?
?
When you see someone suffering or in need, do you feel a sense of responsibility because they are your brother/sister in humanity, or do you dismiss them as 'not my problem'? How does the teaching that 'Adam is from dust' humble you?
?
If you hold any prejudice against a particular group, how can you reconcile that with the clear statement that all humans share the same origin? What concrete action can you take to change your heart?

Praise of Kalam and Purpose of al-Maqasid
The author praises kalam and describes his composition of al-Maqasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's high estimation of kalam and his motivation for writing al-Maqasid, focusing on the revival of neglected topics like sam'iyyat.

Core Idea
Kalam is the noblest science; al-Maqasid revives its highest themes.

In this passage, al-Taftazani opens his commentary with a heartfelt eulogy to the science of kalam. He calls it the firmest of sciences and the most luminous. He then explains why he wrote al-Maqasid: to address topics later theologians neglected, especially the revealed matters (sam'iyyat). This lesson explores the author's purpose and the value he places on his work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the author's own voice.
Notice the poetic and elevated language.
2
Identify the praises of kalam
List the qualities the author attributes to kalam: firmest structure, truest clarification, noblest outcome, etc.
These are meant to establish the importance of the science.
3
Understand the author's description of his work
Note how he describes al-Maqasid and its commentary: concise, precise, with beautiful expressions that ease understanding.
He aims to revive neglected topics.
4
Focus on the sam'iyyat as the ultimate goal
The author singles out sam'iyyat (revealed matters) as the highest objective in theology. Consider why this is important.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text

الشرائع والأحكام، ومقياس قواعد عقائد الإسلام، أعز ما يرغب فيه ويعرج إليه؛ وأهم ما تناخ مطايا الطلب لديه، لكونه أوثق العلوم بنياناً، وأصدقها تبياناً، وأكرمها نتاجاً، وأنورها سراجاً، وأصحها حجة ودليلاً، وأوضحها محجة وسبيلاً، حاموا جميعاً حول طلابه، وراموا طريقاً إلى جنابه، والتمسوا مصباحاً على قبابه ومفتاحاً إلى فتح بابه فافترصت لمعة من ظلم الدهر ونبوة من أنياب النوائب، وانتهزت فرصة من عين الزمان وخفة من زحام الشوائب وأخذت في تصنيف مختصر موسوم بالمقاصد، منظوم فيه غرر الفرائد ودرر الفوائد وشرح له يتضمن بسط موجزه، وحل ملغزه وتفصيل مجمله، وتبيين معضله، مع تحقيق للمقاصد وفق ما يرتاد، وتدقيق للمعاقد فوق ما يعتاد وتحرير للمسائل بحسب ما يراد ولا يزاد وتقرير للدلائل بحيث لا يضاد ولا يصاد، بألفاظ تنفتح بها الآذان وتنشرح الصدور، وتتفطر بالأنهار والأزهار جبال وصخور ، ومعان تهلل بها وجوه الأوراق، وتبتسم ثغور السطور؛ وتتلألأ خلال الكلام وكأنها نور على نور باذلاً الجهد في إيراد مباحث قلت عناية المتأخرين بها من المتكلمين، وقد بالغ في الاعتناء بها المحققون من المتقدمين، لا سيما السمعيات التي هي المطلب الأعلى، والمقصد الأقصى، في أصول الدين والعروة الوثقى، والعمدة القصوى لأهل الحق واليقين.
The laws and rulings, and the measure of the foundations of the beliefs of Islam, the most precious thing desired and sought after; and the most important matter for which the mounts of seeking are made to kneel, because it is the firmest of sciences in structure, the truest in clarification, the noblest in outcome, the most luminous in lamp, the soundest in proof and evidence, and the clearest in path and way. They all gathered around its seekers, and sought a path to its presence, and asked for a lamp upon its dome and a key to open its door. So I seized a gleam from the darkness of time and a protrusion from the fangs of calamities, and I took the opportunity from the eye of the age and the lightness from the crowding of distractions, and I undertook to compose a concise work, called al-Maqasid, containing the choicest pearls and priceless benefits, and a commentary on it that includes spreading its brief, solving its puzzle, detailing its summary, clarifying its difficulty, along with verification of the purposes as desired, and precision of the knots beyond what is usual, and editing the issues according to what is intended and not increased, and establishing the proofs such that they are not opposed nor contradicted, with words by which ears open and hearts expand, and mountains and rocks split with rivers and flowers, and meanings by which the faces of pages smile and the gaps of lines laugh; and they shine through speech as if they were light upon light. I exerted effort in presenting topics which later theologians have neglected, but earlier investigators paid great attention to, especially the sam'iyyat (revealed matters), which are the highest goal and utmost objective in the fundamentals of religion, and the most trustworthy handhold and greatest support for the people of truth and certainty.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a preface in which the author, al-Taftazani, extols the science of kalam and describes his work. He begins by praising the science as the foundation of Islamic beliefs, calling it the most precious and important pursuit. He highlights its superiority over other sciences in structure, clarity, outcome, and evidence. He then recounts his decision to write a concise work titled al-Maqasid, along with a commentary, to address topics that later scholars neglected, especially the sam'iyyat (revealed matters). He describes the style of his work: it will be clear, precise, and comprehensive, with beautiful expressions that ease understanding. The author emphasizes that he has focused on the highest goals of theology.

المقاصد (al-Maqāṣid)
عقائد (ʿaqāʾid)

Correction: The author is establishing the importance of kalam and his work in reviving neglected topics, not self-aggrandizement.

What Is Kalam?

The Two Powers and the Parallel between Kalam and Philosophy
Sanusi explains the two human powers and aligns kalam with theoretical philosophy under revelatory guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that kalam corresponds to theoretical philosophy in perfecting the theoretical power of the soul, but with the crucial difference that the intellect in kalam follows divine guidance.

Core Idea
Kalam perfects the theoretical power under revelation's guidance.

In this opening passage of his commentary, Sanusi sets the stage for his entire work by explaining the two perfections that every human soul seeks: theoretical and practical. He then draws a direct parallel between the science of kalam and philosophy, showing how both aim to perfect these powers, but with a fundamental difference in the source of guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two powers
Read the passage and note the definitions of theoretical and practical power. Their perfections are key to understanding the rest.
Theoretical power's perfection is knowledge of realities; practical power's perfection is proper action.
2
Compare kalam and philosophy
Observe how Sanusi says religion and philosophy both aim to perfect these powers, but the intellect in religion follows guidance while in philosophy it follows whim.
This is the central distinction that sets kalam apart.
3
Examine the branches of philosophy
See how theoretical philosophy is divided into divine, mathematical, and natural. Note which branch corresponds to the main concerns of kalam.
Kalam focuses on the divine and the study of contingent things to know the beginning and the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يحول أيسرها بين المرء وقلبه، وتصدأ به مرآة فكره وعقله، ويزول بأدونها ريق خاطره وناظره، ويذهب رونق باطنه وظاهره، إلى أن تداركني نعمة من ربي، وتماسك بي عودة من فهمي ولبي، فأقبلت على إتمام الكتاب وانتظام تلك الفصول والأبواب، فجاء بحمد الله كنزاً مدفوناً من جواهر الفرائد، وبحراً مشحوناً بنفائس الفوائد، في لطائف طالما كانت مخزونة، وعن الإضاعة مصونة مع تنقيح للكلام وتوضيح للمرام، بتقريرات ترتاح لها نفوس المحصلين، وينزاح منها شبه المبطلين، وتضيء أنوارها في قلوب الطالبين، وتطلع نيرانها على أفئدة الحاسدين، لا يعقل بيناتها إلا العالمون، ولا يجحد بآياتها إلا القوم الظالمون، يهتز لها علماء البلاد، في كل ناد، ولا يغض منها إلا كل هائم في واد، من يهد الله فهو المهتد ومن يضلله فما له من هاد، وإذا قرع سمعك ما لم تسمع به من الأولين، فلا تسرع وقف وقفة المتأملين، لعلك تطلع بوميض برق إلهي، وتألق نور رباني، من شاطيء الوادي الأيمن في البقعة المباركة على برهان له جلي، أو بيان من آخرين واضح خفي، والله سبحانه وتعالى ولي الإعانة والتوفيق وبتحقيق آمال المؤمنين حقيق.

قال: ورتبته على ستة مقاصد

أقول: اعلم أن للإنسان قوة نظرية كما لها معرفة الحقائق كما هي، وعملية كمالها القيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي تحصيلاً لسعادة الدارين، وقد تطابقت الملة والفلسفة على الاعتناء بتكميل النفوس البشرية في القوتين وتسهيل طريق الوصول إلى الغايتين، إلا أن نظر العقل يتبع في الملة هداه وفي الفلسفة هواه، وكما دونت حكماء الفلاسفة الحكمة النظرية والعلمية إعانة للعامة على تحصيل الكمالات المتعلقة بالقوتين دونت عظماء الملة وعلماء الأمة علم الكلام، وعلم الشرائع والأحكام، فوقع الكلام للملة بإزاء الحكمة النظرية للفلسفة وهي عندهم تنقسم إلى العلم المتعلق بأمور تستغني عن المادة في الورود والتصور جميعاً، وهو الإلهي أو في التصور فقط وهو الرياضي، أو لا تستغني أصلاً وهي الطبيعي ولكل منها أقسام وفروع كثيرة، إلا أن المقدم في الاعتبار بشهادة العقل والنقل هو معرفة المبدأ و المعاد المشار إليهما بالإيمان بالله تعالى واليوم الآخر، وطريق الوصول إليها هو النظر في الممكنات من الجواهر والأعراض على ما يرشد إليه مواضع من كتاب الله تعالى وما أحسن ما أشار أمير المؤمنين علي كرم الله وجهه إلى أن المعتبر من كمال القوة العملية ما به نظام المعاش، ونجاة المعاد ومن النظرية العلم بالمبدأ والمعاد وما بينهما من جهة النظر والاعتبار حيث قال: رحم الله امرءاً أخذ لنفسه، واستعد لرمسه، وعلم من أين وفي أين وإلى أين؟
What removes the smallest of them between a person and his heart, and rusts with it the mirror of his thought and intellect, and with the least of them the saliva of his imagination and sight disappears, and the luster of his inner and outer being goes away, until a grace from my Lord overtook me, and a return of my understanding and mind held me together. So I set about completing the book and arranging those chapters and sections, and it came, by the grace of God, as a hidden treasure of the jewels of unique gems, and a sea filled with precious benefits, in subtleties that were long stored and protected from loss, with refinement of speech and clarification of the goal, with determinations that the souls of students find restful, and by which the doubts of the falsifiers are removed, and its lights shine in the hearts of seekers, and its fires blaze upon the hearts of the envious. None comprehend its clear proofs except the knowledgeable, and none deny its verses except the wrongdoing people. The scholars of the land are stirred by it in every assembly, and none disparage it except every one wandering in a valley. Whom God guides is guided, and whom He leads astray has no guide. And if what you have not heard from the earlier ones strikes your ear, do not hasten; pause the pause of the contemplative, perhaps you will glimpse a flash of divine lightning and a gleam of lordly light from the bank of the right valley in the blessed spot upon a clear proof or a manifest hidden explanation from others. God, exalted and high, is the possessor of assistance and success, and He is worthy of fulfilling the hopes of the believers.

He said: And I arranged it into six purposes.

I say: Know that man has a theoretical power whose perfection is the knowledge of realities as they are, and a practical power whose perfection is the performance of affairs in the proper manner to attain the happiness of the two abodes. And religion and philosophy have agreed on the care for perfecting human souls in both powers and facilitating the way to the two ends, except that the consideration of the intellect in religion follows its guidance, while in philosophy it follows its whim. And just as the philosophers of wisdom have recorded theoretical and practical wisdom to help the common people to attain the perfections related to the two powers, so the great scholars of religion and the learned of the nation have recorded the science of kalam and the science of sharia and rulings. Thus, kalam for religion corresponds to theoretical wisdom for philosophy. And philosophy among them is divided into the science that is independent of matter both in occurrence and conception, which is the divine; or independent only in conception, which is the mathematical; or not independent at all, which is the natural. Each has many divisions and branches. However, the most important, by the testimony of intellect and transmission, is the knowledge of the beginning and the end, which are indicated by belief in God Almighty and the Last Day. And the way to attain them is inquiry into contingent things, both substances and accidents, as indicated by passages from the Book of God Almighty. How excellent is what the Commander of the Faithful Ali—may God honor his face—pointed out that what is considered from the perfection of the practical power is that by which the order of livelihood and the salvation of the final abode are achieved, and from the theoretical power is the knowledge of the beginning and the end and what is between them from the perspective of inquiry and reflection, when he said: May God have mercy on a person who takes for himself, prepares for his grave, and knows from where, in where, and to where?
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an introduction in which the author first praises God for enabling him to complete the book, describing it as a treasure of gems and a sea of benefits that delights scholars and refutes falsifiers. He then quotes a statement of arrangement: 'He said: And I arranged it into six purposes'—referring to the six major sections of the work. The author's commentary begins with 'I say: Know that man has a theoretical power...' He explains that humans possess two powers: theoretical (al-quwwa an-nazariyya) whose perfection is knowledge of realities as they are, and practical (al-quwwa al-‘amaliyya) whose perfection is proper action to achieve happiness in this world and the next. He notes that both religion and philosophy agree on perfecting these powers, but the intellect in religion follows guidance, while in philosophy it follows whim. He then draws a parallel: just as philosophers wrote theoretical and practical wisdom, the great scholars of the Muslim community wrote the science of kalam and the science of sharia and rulings. Thus, kalam corresponds to theoretical philosophy from the perspective of religion. He then defines the three branches of theoretical philosophy: divine (al-ilahi) independent of matter in both occurrence and conception; mathematical (ar-riyadi) independent only in conception; and natural (at-tabi‘i) not independent at all. Among these, the most important is knowledge of the beginning (al-mabda') and the end (al-ma‘ad), which are belief in God and the Last Day. The way to attain this knowledge is by inquiry (nazar) into contingent things (mumkinat) such as substances and accidents, guided by Quranic verses. He ends with a quote from Ali ibn Abi Talib emphasizing that the perfection of the practical power achieves worldly order and salvation, while the theoretical power achieves knowledge of the beginning and the end.

Key Terms
قوة نظرية
قوة عملية
كلام
فلسفة
مبدأ
معاد
نظر
ممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam is a purely rational discipline like philosophy, independent of revelation.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the intellect in kalam follows guidance (هدى) from revelation, while philosophy follows whim (هوى). So kalam is guided by revealed truths.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the relationship between kalam and philosophy differ in terms of the guidance of the intellect?
A) In philosophy the intellect follows guidance; in kalam it follows whim.
B) In kalam the intellect follows guidance; in philosophy it follows whim.
C) Both kalam and philosophy follow the same guidance.
D) Neither kalam nor philosophy uses the intellect.

Key Takeaway
Kalam corresponds to theoretical philosophy in aim but is distinguished by its reliance on divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge, do you honestly rely more on divine guidance (such as the Quran and prophetic teachings) or on your own opinions and desires? In what specific area of your life has 'hawa' (whim) led you astray, and what will you do to return to 'huda' (guidance)?
?
The passage teaches that perfecting your theoretical power means knowing the beginning and the end—your origin and destiny. Have you truly pondered these realities? If you knew you would stand before God soon, what change would you make today in your pursuit of knowledge?
?
Ali (ra) said: 'Know from where, in where, and to where.' Do you live each day mindful of your purpose—why you are here, what you are doing, and where you are going? Write down one concrete step you will take tomorrow to align your actions with that awareness.

The Shared Goal and Distinct Path of Kalam and Philosophy
Author compares kalam and philosophy, noting their common aim but different guides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam and philosophy both seek human perfection but differ fundamentally in the role of divine guidance versus independent reason.

Core Idea
Kalam follows divine light; philosophy follows its own light.

This passage begins by establishing a startling point of agreement: religion (milla) and philosophy (falsafa) both aim to perfect the human soul. But then the author draws a sharp line: in religion, reason is guided by revelation; in philosophy, reason follows its own desire. This comparison sets the stage for defining kalam as the Islamic counterpart to philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening comparison
Read the first few lines where the author states the common goal of milla and philosophy.
Notice the word 'تطابقت' – they agreed.
2
Identify the critical difference
Look for the contrast between 'هداه' (its guidance) and 'هواه' (its desire). This is the key distinction.
3
Note the parallel authorship
The author says that just as philosophers wrote books, great men of the community wrote kalam and law.
This shows kalam is the Islamic equivalent of theoretical philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي تحصيلاً لسعادة الدارين، وقد تطابقت الملة والفلسفة على الاعتناء بتكميل النفوس البشرية في القوتين وتسهيل طريق الوصول إلى الغايتين، إلا أن نظر العقل يتبع في الملة هداه وفي الفلسفة هواه ، وكما دونت حكماء الفلاسفة الحكمة النظرية والعلمية إعانة للعامة على تحصيل الكمالات المتعلقة بالقوتين دونت عظماء الملة وعلماء الأمة علم الكلام ، وعلم الشرائع والأحكام ، فوقع الكلام للملة (۱) للملة(۱) بإزاء الحكمة النظرية للفلسفة وهي عندهم تنقسم إلى العلم المتعلق (۲) بأمور تستغني عن المادة في الورود والتصور جميعاً ، وهو الإلهي أو في التصور فقط وهو الرياضي، أو لا تستغني أصلاً وهي الطبيعي ولكل منها أقسام وفروع كثيرة، إلا أن المقدم في الاعتبار بشهادة العقل والنقل هو معرفة المبدأ و المعاد المشار إليهما بالإيمان بالله تعالى واليوم الآخر، وطريق الوصول إليها هو النظر في الممكنات (۳) من الجواهر والأعراض على ما يرشد إليه (٤) مواضع من كتاب الله تعالى وما أحسن ما أشار أمير المؤمنين علي كرم الله وجهه إلى أن المعتبر من كمال القوة العملية ما به نظام المعاش، ونجاة المعاد ومن النظرية العلم بالمبدأ والمعاد وما بينهما من جهة النظر والاعتبار حيث قال : لرحم الله امرءاً أخذ لنفسه، واستعد لرمسه، وعلم من أين وفي أين وإلى أين؟ (۱) الملة كالدين وهي ما شرع الله للعباد على لسان المرسلين ليتوصلوا به إلى جوار الله، والفرق بينها وبين الدين أن الملة لا تضاف إلا إلى النبي الذي تستند إليه نحو (فاتبعوا ملة إبراهيم ولا تكاد توجد مضافة إلى لفظ الله تعالى ولا إلى آحاد أمة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولا تستعمل إلا في جملة الشرائع دون آحادها لا يقال ملة الله ولا ملتي ولا ملة زيد، كما يقال دين الله وديني، ودين زيد ولا يقال للصلاة ملة الله . كما يقال دين الله . (۲) سقط من (أ) كلمة (بأمور). (۳) يطلق النظر على معان فيراد به الرؤية والإحسان والرحمة ونظر القلب، وهو المقصود هنا لأنه يقوم على التفكير الذي يكشف عن الحقيقة وقد دعا الله إليه أو لم ينظروا في ملكوت السموات والأرض وفي الأرض آيات للموقنين) (وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون). وهو واجب عند المسلمين جميعاً ويعول عليه المعتزلة كل التعويل لأن العقل قبل السمع. وكان الناس قبل ورود الشرائع يحتكمون إلى عقولهم فيحسنون ويقبحون، وما دام النظر واجباً فالتقليد فاسد ويؤدي إلى جحد الضرورة. (راجع المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار ص د هـ والمقدمة ج (۱۲). (٤) في (ب) على ما يرشد مواضع إليه .
The undertaking of affairs as they ought to be for the attainment of happiness in both abodes. And religion (al-milla) and philosophy (al-falsafa) have agreed upon caring for the perfection of human souls in both faculties and facilitating the path of reaching both ends. However, the view of reason follows in religion its guidance, and in philosophy its desire. And just as the sages of the philosophers wrote down theoretical and practical wisdom (al-hikma al-nazariyya wa al-'amaliyya) to aid the common people in attaining the perfections related to the two faculties, the great men of religion and the scholars of the community wrote down the science of kalam (ilm al-kalam) and the science of laws and rulings (ilm al-shara'i' wa al-ahkam). So kalam came for religion corresponding to theoretical wisdom for philosophy. And theoretical philosophy according to them is divided into the science concerned with matters that are independent of matter in both existence and conception—that is the divine (al-ilahi); or only in conception—that is the mathematical (al-riyadi); or not independent at all—that is the natural (al-tabi'i). And each has many divisions and branches. However, the most important consideration, by the testimony of intellect and revelation, is knowledge of the beginning (al-mabda') and the return (al-ma'ad) which are indicated by faith in God Most High and the Last Day. And the way to reach them is by examining possible things (al-mumkinat) from substances (al-jawahir) and accidents (al-a'rad) according to what is indicated by passages from the Book of God Most High. And how excellent is the indication of the Commander of the Faithful, Ali (may God honor his face), that what is considered from the perfection of the practical faculty (al-quwwa al-'amaliyya) is that by which the order of livelihood and salvation in the afterlife is established, and from the theoretical faculty (al-nazariyya) is knowledge of the beginning and the return and what is between them from the aspect of reflection and consideration. He said: 'May God have mercy on a person who takes provision for himself, and prepares for his grave, and knows from where, in where, and to where.' (1) Al-milla is like al-din (religion), and it is what God prescribed for servants upon the tongues of the messengers that they may attain nearness to God. The difference between it and al-din is that al-milla is attributed only to the prophet to whom it is traced, like 'Follow the milla of Abraham'; it is hardly ever attributed to the word God Most High nor to individuals of the nation of the Prophet (peace be upon him); it is used only for the totality of laws, not for individual ones. One does not say 'milla of God' nor 'my milla' nor 'the milla of Zayd', just as one says 'din of God', 'my din', and 'din of Zayd'; nor is 'milla of God' said for prayer as 'din of God' is said. (2) In copy (أ) the word (بأمور) is missing. (3) Al-nazar (reflection) is used for various meanings: sight, favor, mercy, and the reflection of the heart; the intended meaning here is that which is based on thinking that unveils truth. God has called to it: 'Do they not look at the dominion of the heavens and the earth?' and 'In the earth are signs for those who are certain' and 'In yourselves, do you not see?' It is obligatory according to all Muslims, and the Mu'tazila rely on it completely because reason precedes revelation. People before the coming of laws used to refer to their intellects, deeming things good and bad. As long as reflection is obligatory, taqlid (blind following) is corrupt and leads to denial of the necessary. (Refer to al-Mughni by Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, page d h, and al-Muqaddima, vol. 12). (4) In copy (ب): 'according to what (مواضع) indicates to him'.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction, comparing the science of kalam to philosophy and defining key terms. The author begins by stating the common aim of both religion (milla) and philosophy: the perfection of the human soul to achieve happiness in both this world and the Hereafter. However, he immediately notes a critical distinction: in religion, the intellect follows divine guidance (huda), whereas in philosophy it follows its own desire (hawa). This sets kalam apart as a discipline that is rooted in revelation. The author then draws a parallel: just as philosophers produced works on theoretical and practical wisdom, Islamic scholars authored works on kalam and jurisprudence (fiqh). Kalam is positioned as the Islamic counterpart to theoretical philosophy. To clarify this, the author outlines the standard division of theoretical philosophy into three branches: divine science (al-ilahi), which deals with matters completely independent of matter; mathematical science (al-riyadi), which is independent only in conception; and natural science (al-tabi'i), which is dependent on matter in both existence and conception. Despite this broad division, the author emphasizes that the most important subject, attested by both reason and revelation, is knowledge of the Beginning (al-mabda') and the Return (al-ma'ad)—that is, God and the afterlife. The path to this knowledge is through reflection (nazar) on possible beings, specifically substances and accidents, guided by verses of the Quran. To illustrate the dual perfection, the author quotes Ali ibn Abi Talib, who states that the perfection of the practical faculty lies in establishing worldly order and ensuring salvation in the afterlife, while the perfection of the theoretical faculty is knowledge of the beginning, the end, and what lies between them. Finally, the passage includes extensive footnotes. The first footnote defines 'milla' as a religious community linked to a specific prophet, distinguishing it from 'din' (religion) which can be used more broadly. The third footnote explains 'nazar' (reflection) in detail, emphasizing its obligation and its role in rational inquiry, noting that the Mu'tazila heavily rely on it, and that taqlid (blind following) is invalid. These footnotes provide crucial terminological and methodological clarifications that underpin the rest of the work.

Key Terms
الملة
الفلسفة
النظر
القوتين
الغايتين
هدى
هوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kalam is just Islamic philosophy and uses the same method.
Correction: The author clarifies that kalam is guided by revelation (huda), unlike philosophy which follows desire (hawa).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main difference between religion (milla) and philosophy in seeking human perfection?
A) Religion uses reason while philosophy uses revelation.
B) Religion follows divine guidance while philosophy follows desire.
C) Both are identical.
D) Philosophy is superior.

Key Takeaway
Kalam and philosophy share a common goal, but kalam is distinguished by its submission to divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make decisions in your daily life, do you follow divine guidance (huda) or your own desires (hawa)? Can you identify one area where you have been following your desire instead of what Allah has revealed? What will you change today?
?
The author says kalam was written to aid the common people. How are you using the science of kalam (or any Islamic knowledge) to perfect your own soul? Are you actively seeking knowledge that aligns your reason with revelation, or are you content with intellectual laziness?
?
If reason follows its desire in philosophy, do you ever find yourself rationalizing sins using your own logic? How can you ensure your reasoning is guided by the light of the Quran and Sunnah, not by your whims?

The Three Branches of Theoretical Philosophy
Author enumerates the three branches of theoretical philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold division of theoretical philosophy (divine, mathematical, natural) and how kalam aligns with the divine branch.

Core Idea
Theoretical philosophy is divided into the divine, the mathematical, and the natural.

Having positioned kalam as the counterpart to theoretical philosophy, the author now defines what theoretical philosophy is. He explains its three branches based on their relationship to matter: those completely independent, those independent only in conception, and those dependent. This classification helps situate kalam as the 'divine science' that deals with immaterial realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of theoretical philosophy
Read the paragraph where the author divides theoretical philosophy into three branches.
Pay attention to the criteria: independence from matter in existence and conception.
2
Identify the three branches
List the three branches: divine, mathematical, natural. Note how each relates to matter.
3
Connect to kalam
Think about why kalam corresponds to the divine branch (al-ilahi).
Kalam discusses God and the afterlife, which are immaterial.

Checkpoint
Which branch of theoretical philosophy is independent of matter in both existence and conception?
A) Divine (al-ilahi)
B) Mathematical (al-riyadi)

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
According to this passage, the divine branch is concerned with matters independent of matter in both existence and conception. Which branch do you spend most of your intellectual energy on? If it is not the divine, what does that reveal about your priorities in light of this classification? Will you commit to dedicating a portion of your study time this week to learning about matters that transcend the material?
?
The mathematical is said to be independent only in conception, not in existence. This means that even abstract numbers and shapes ultimately rely on material reality. Do you place more certainty in mathematical proofs than in the immaterial truths of God and the afterlife? Where does your heart truly rest for ultimate certainty?

The Way to Knowledge: Reflection (Nazar) and the Wisdom of Ali
Author explains the method of nazar and quotes Ali on perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the path to knowing God and the afterlife is through reflection (nazar) on the physical world, as exemplified by the wisdom of Ali.

Core Idea
Reflection on creation is the path to knowing the Creator.

The author now turns to the method of attaining the true goal: knowledge of the Beginning and the Return. He emphasizes that this is achieved through nazar (reflection) on possible beings (substances and accidents), guided by Quranic verses. He then supports this with a profound statement from Ali that encapsulates the dual perfection of practical and theoretical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the method of knowledge
Read from 'إلا أن المقدم في الاعتبار' to the end of the quote of Ali.
Notice the emphasis on reflection (nazar) and the Quranic guidance.
2
Understand the two perfections from Ali's quote
Ali mentions practical perfection (worldly order and afterlife salvation) and theoretical perfection (knowledge of beginning and end).
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'from where, in where, to where'
These three questions summarize the existential inquiry: origin, present state, and destination.

القيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي تحصيلاً لسعادة الدارين، وقد تطابقت الملة والفلسفة على الاعتناء بتكميل النفوس البشرية في القوتين وتسهيل طريق الوصول إلى الغايتين، إلا أن نظر العقل يتبع في الملة هداه وفي الفلسفة هواه ، وكما دونت حكماء الفلاسفة الحكمة النظرية والعلمية إعانة للعامة على تحصيل الكمالات المتعلقة بالقوتين دونت عظماء الملة وعلماء الأمة علم الكلام ، وعلم الشرائع والأحكام ، فوقع الكلام للملة (۱) للملة(۱) بإزاء الحكمة النظرية للفلسفة وهي عندهم تنقسم إلى العلم المتعلق (۲) بأمور تستغني عن المادة في الورود والتصور جميعاً ، وهو الإلهي أو في التصور فقط وهو الرياضي، أو لا تستغني أصلاً وهي الطبيعي ولكل منها أقسام وفروع كثيرة، إلا أن المقدم في الاعتبار بشهادة العقل والنقل هو معرفة المبدأ و المعاد المشار إليهما بالإيمان بالله تعالى واليوم الآخر، وطريق الوصول إليها هو النظر في الممكنات (۳) من الجواهر والأعراض على ما يرشد إليه (٤) مواضع من كتاب الله تعالى وما أحسن ما أشار أمير المؤمنين علي كرم الله وجهه إلى أن المعتبر من كمال القوة العملية ما به نظام المعاش، ونجاة المعاد ومن النظرية العلم بالمبدأ والمعاد وما بينهما من جهة النظر والاعتبار حيث قال : لرحم الله امرءاً أخذ لنفسه، واستعد لرمسه، وعلم من أين وفي أين وإلى أين؟ (۱) الملة كالدين وهي ما شرع الله للعباد على لسان المرسلين ليتوصلوا به إلى جوار الله، والفرق بينها وبين الدين أن الملة لا تضاف إلا إلى النبي الذي تستند إليه نحو (فاتبعوا ملة إبراهيم ولا تكاد توجد مضافة إلى لفظ الله تعالى ولا إلى آحاد أمة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولا تستعمل إلا في جملة الشرائع دون آحادها لا يقال ملة الله ولا ملتي ولا ملة زيد، كما يقال دين الله وديني، ودين زيد ولا يقال للصلاة ملة الله . كما يقال دين الله . (۲) سقط من (أ) كلمة (بأمور). (۳) يطلق النظر على معان فيراد به الرؤية والإحسان والرحمة ونظر القلب، وهو المقصود هنا لأنه يقوم على التفكير الذي يكشف عن الحقيقة وقد دعا الله إليه أو لم ينظروا في ملكوت السموات والأرض وفي الأرض آيات للموقنين) (وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون). وهو واجب عند المسلمين جميعاً ويعول عليه المعتزلة كل التعويل لأن العقل قبل السمع. وكان الناس قبل ورود الشرائع يحتكمون إلى عقولهم فيحسنون ويقبحون، وما دام النظر واجباً فالتقليد فاسد ويؤدي إلى جحد الضرورة. (راجع المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار ص د هـ والمقدمة ج (۱۲). (٤) في (ب) على ما يرشد مواضع إليه .
The undertaking of affairs as they ought to be for the attainment of happiness in both abodes. And religion (al-milla) and philosophy (al-falsafa) have agreed upon caring for the perfection of human souls in both faculties and facilitating the path of reaching both ends. However, the view of reason follows in religion its guidance, and in philosophy its desire. And just as the sages of the philosophers wrote down theoretical and practical wisdom (al-hikma al-nazariyya wa al-'amaliyya) to aid the common people in attaining the perfections related to the two faculties, the great men of religion and the scholars of the community wrote down the science of kalam (ilm al-kalam) and the science of laws and rulings (ilm al-shara'i' wa al-ahkam). So kalam came for religion corresponding to theoretical wisdom for philosophy. And theoretical philosophy according to them is divided into the science concerned with matters that are independent of matter in both existence and conception—that is the divine (al-ilahi); or only in conception—that is the mathematical (al-riyadi); or not independent at all—that is the natural (al-tabi'i). And each has many divisions and branches. However, the most important consideration, by the testimony of intellect and revelation, is knowledge of the beginning (al-mabda') and the return (al-ma'ad) which are indicated by faith in God Most High and the Last Day. And the way to reach them is by examining possible things (al-mumkinat) from substances (al-jawahir) and accidents (al-a'rad) according to what is indicated by passages from the Book of God Most High. And how excellent is the indication of the Commander of the Faithful, Ali (may God honor his face), that what is considered from the perfection of the practical faculty (al-quwwa al-'amaliyya) is that by which the order of livelihood and salvation in the afterlife is established, and from the theoretical faculty (al-nazariyya) is knowledge of the beginning and the return and what is between them from the aspect of reflection and consideration. He said: 'May God have mercy on a person who takes provision for himself, and prepares for his grave, and knows from where, in where, and to where.' (1) Al-milla is like al-din (religion), and it is what God prescribed for servants upon the tongues of the messengers that they may attain nearness to God. The difference between it and al-din is that al-milla is attributed only to the prophet to whom it is traced, like 'Follow the milla of Abraham'; it is hardly ever attributed to the word God Most High nor to individuals of the nation of the Prophet (peace be upon him); it is used only for the totality of laws, not for individual ones. One does not say 'milla of God' nor 'my milla' nor 'the milla of Zayd', just as one says 'din of God', 'my din', and 'din of Zayd'; nor is 'milla of God' said for prayer as 'din of God' is said. (2) In copy (أ) the word (بأمور) is missing. (3) Al-nazar (reflection) is used for various meanings: sight, favor, mercy, and the reflection of the heart; the intended meaning here is that which is based on thinking that unveils truth. God has called to it: 'Do they not look at the dominion of the heavens and the earth?' and 'In the earth are signs for those who are certain' and 'In yourselves, do you not see?' It is obligatory according to all Muslims, and the Mu'tazila rely on it completely because reason precedes revelation. People before the coming of laws used to refer to their intellects, deeming things good and bad. As long as reflection is obligatory, taqlid (blind following) is corrupt and leads to denial of the necessary. (Refer to al-Mughni by Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, page d h, and al-Muqaddima, vol. 12). (4) In copy (ب): 'according to what (مواضع) indicates to him'.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction, comparing the science of kalam to philosophy and defining key terms. The author begins by stating the common aim of both religion (milla) and philosophy: the perfection of the human soul to achieve happiness in both this world and the Hereafter. However, he immediately notes a critical distinction: in religion, the intellect follows divine guidance (huda), whereas in philosophy it follows its own desire (hawa). This sets kalam apart as a discipline that is rooted in revelation. The author then draws a parallel: just as philosophers produced works on theoretical and practical wisdom, Islamic scholars authored works on kalam and jurisprudence (fiqh). Kalam is positioned as the Islamic counterpart to theoretical philosophy. To clarify this, the author outlines the standard division of theoretical philosophy into three branches: divine science (al-ilahi), which deals with matters completely independent of matter; mathematical science (al-riyadi), which is independent only in conception; and natural science (al-tabi'i), which is dependent on matter in both existence and conception. Despite this broad division, the author emphasizes that the most important subject, attested by both reason and revelation, is knowledge of the Beginning (al-mabda') and the Return (al-ma'ad)—that is, God and the afterlife. The path to this knowledge is through reflection (nazar) on possible beings, specifically substances and accidents, guided by verses of the Quran. To illustrate the dual perfection, the author quotes Ali ibn Abi Talib, who states that the perfection of the practical faculty lies in establishing worldly order and ensuring salvation in the afterlife, while the perfection of the theoretical faculty is knowledge of the beginning, the end, and what lies between them. Finally, the passage includes extensive footnotes. The first footnote defines 'milla' as a religious community linked to a specific prophet, distinguishing it from 'din' (religion) which can be used more broadly. The third footnote explains 'nazar' (reflection) in detail, emphasizing its obligation and its role in rational inquiry, noting that the Mu'tazila heavily rely on it, and that taqlid (blind following) is invalid. These footnotes provide crucial terminological and methodological clarifications that underpin the rest of the work.

Key Terms
المبدأ
المعاد
النظر
الممكنات
الجواهر
الأعراض
القوة العملية
القوة النظرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think nazar (reflection) is just speculation without evidence.
Correction: The passage states nazar is based on examining actual things (substances and accidents) and guided by revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the way to attain knowledge of the Beginning and the Return?
A) Blind faith
B) Reflection (nazar) on possible things
C) Mere philosophy
D) Following desire

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God and the afterlife is attained through disciplined reflection (nazar) on the created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you—trees, stars, your own body—do you habitually see them as signs pointing to the Creator, or are you distracted by their immediate appeal? What one habit can you start today to train your theoretical faculty to see beyond the surface?
?
Ali said we should know 'from where, in where, to where.' Have you honestly confronted these questions for yourself? What is your ultimate destiny, and what provision are you sending ahead to your grave right now?
?
The practical faculty is for establishing livelihood and salvation. Are you more absorbed in securing your worldly life than in securing your afterlife? If you were to die tonight, would your theoretical and practical faculties have succeeded in their purposes? What is the most urgent change you need to make?

Definition of Milla and its Distinction from Din
Author defines milla and distinguishes it from din.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'milla' as a religious community tied to a prophet, and how it differs from 'din'.

Core Idea
Milla is the path of a prophet; din is a broader term for religion.

The author provides a detailed lexical and technical definition of the term 'milla', distinguishing it from the more general term 'din'. This clarification is essential for understanding the specific sense in which kalam is associated with the milla of Islam, as opposed to philosophy or other religious traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of milla
Read the footnote that defines milla as what God prescribed through messengers.
Note that milla is always linked to a specific prophet.
2
Understand the distinction from din
See how milla cannot be used for God or individuals, while din can.
Examples: 'milla of Ibrahim' vs 'din of Allah'.
3
Apply the distinction
Think of why the author uses 'milla' rather than 'din' in the comparison with philosophy.
Milla emphasizes the prophetic origin.

?

What Is Kalam?

The Definition of Ilm al-Kalam
The author defines ilm al-kalam and explains the distinction between ittisaf and tasawwur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of ilm al-kalam and the difference between possessing a quality and merely conceptualizing it.

Core Idea
Ilm al-kalam is knowledge of religious beliefs based on certain proofs, not mere talk.

This lesson introduces the formal definition of the science of kalam. The author begins by stating that kalam is knowledge of religious beliefs derived from certain proofs. He then clarifies a subtle but important distinction between having a quality in reality and merely conceptualizing it, which underlies the structure of all sciences, including kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Read the Arabic definition of ilm al-kalam and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'certain proofs' (al-adilla al-yaqiniyya).
The definition is concise but foundational.
2
Understand the two types of psychological attainment
The author explains that a quality can be possessed actually (ittisaf) or conceptualized (tasawwur). Use the example of generosity to see the difference.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of knowledge.
3
Grasp the nature of every science
The author states that every science consists of conceptualizations (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat) sought through reasoning. This applies to kalam as well.
This shows why reasoning and inference are central to kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف علم الكلام قال : الكلام) هو العلم بالعقائد الدينية عن الأدلة اليقينية) أقول : حصول الكيفيات فسانية في النفس قد تكون بأعيانها وهو اتصاف بها ، وقد تكون بصورها وهو تصور لها كالكريم يتصف بالكرم وإن لم يتصوره ، وغير الكريم يتصوره وإن لم يتصف به، ولا خفاء في أن حقيقة كل علم من الكلام وغيره تصورات وتصديقات كثيرة يطلب حصولها بأعيانها بطريق النظر والاستدلال، فاحتيج إلى ما يفيد
The definition of the science of kalam: He said: 'Kalam is knowledge of religious beliefs from certain proofs.' I say: The attainment of psychological qualities in the soul may be in their very selves, which is being characterized by them, or be in their forms, which is conceptualizing them—like the generous person who is characterized by generosity even if he does not conceptualize it, and the non-generous person who conceptualizes it even if he is not characterized by it. There is no doubt that the reality of every science, whether kalam or other, consists of many conceptualizations and assents that are sought to be attained in their very selves by way of reasoning and inference, so there is need for something that benefits...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of ilm al-kalam as 'knowledge of religious beliefs from certain proofs' (al-ilm bi al-aqa'id al-diniyya an al-adilla al-yaqiniyya). The author then begins explaining the nature of knowledge in the soul. He distinguishes between the actual possession of qualities (ittisaf) and the conceptualization (tasawwur) of them, using the example of generosity: a generous person is characterized by it even without conceptualizing it, while a non-generous person can conceptualize it without being characterized by it. This leads to the point that every science, including kalam, consists of many conceptualizations and assents (tasdiqat) that are sought to be attained in themselves through reasoning and inference. Therefore, something is needed that provides benefit... (the text cuts off). The definition highlights that kalam is a science (ilm) based on certain proofs, not mere opinion or blind acceptance.

Correction: Ilm al-kalam is a specific science defined as knowledge of religious beliefs derived from certain proofs, not mere discussion.

The Definition of ʿIlm al-Kalām
Al-Sanusi defines kalām and explains the function of a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ʿilm al-kalām and the purpose of a scientific definition as explained by al-Sanusi.

Core Idea
A definition guides inquiry by providing an overall conceptualization of a science.

Al-Sanusi begins by stating the definition of kalām: knowledge of religious beliefs based on certain proofs. He then explains that a definition is not merely a label but serves to give a general picture of the science, keeping the student focused on what belongs to it. This lesson unpacks that definition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic sentence 'الكلام هو العلم بالعقائد الدينية عن الأدلة اليقينية' and its translation. Notice the key terms: ʿilm, ʿaqāʾid dīniyya, adilla yaqīniyya.
This is the core definition of kalām.
2
Understand the ittiṣāf/taṣawwur distinction
The author distinguishes between actually possessing a quality (ittiṣāf) and merely having a mental concept of it (taṣawwur). Consider the example of generosity: a generous person is characterized by generosity even without conceptualizing it, while a stingy person may conceptualize generosity without being generous.
This shows that a science is not just a set of concepts but actual acquired qualities.
3
Grasp the purpose of a definition
The author explains that every science consists of many conceptualizations and affirmations sought through reasoning. A definition provides a general conceptualization that guides inquiry, preventing the student from falling short or engaging with irrelevant matters.
Definitions are often omitted in religious sciences because their topics are compiled and learned directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف علم الكلام. قال: الكلام هو العلم بالعقائد الدينية عن الأدلة اليقينية. أقول: حصول الكيفيات النفسانية في النفس قد تكون بأعيانها وهو اتصاف بها، وقد تكون بصورها وهو تصور لها كالكريم يتصف بالكرم وإن لم يتصوره، وغير الكريم يتصوره وإن لم يتصف به، ولا خفاء في أن حقيقة كل علم من الكلام وغيره تصورات وتصديقات كثيرة يطلب حصولها بأعيانها بطريق النظر والاستدلال، فاحتيج إلى ما يفيد تصورها بصورة إجمالية تساويها صوناً للطلب والنظر عن إخلال بما هو واشتغال بما ليس منها، وذلك هو المعنى بتعريف العلم، فكان وإنما كثر تركه سيما في العلوم الشرعية والأدبية لما شاع من تدوين العلوم بمسائلها ودلائلها، وتفسير ما يتعلق بها من التصورات ثم تحصيلها كذلك بطريق التعلم من العلم، أو التفهم من الكتاب. وإذا تقرر هذا فنقول: الأحكام المنسوبة إلى الشرع منها ما يتعلق بالعمل وتسمى فرعية وعملية ومنها ما يتعلق بالاعتقاد، وتسمى أصلية واعتقادية.
Definition of the science of kalām. He said: Kalām is the knowledge of religious beliefs based on certain proofs. I say: The acquisition of psychic qualities in the soul may be by their very selves, which is being characterized by them, or by their forms, which is conceptualizing them—like the generous person who is characterized by generosity even if he does not conceptualize it, and the non-generous person who conceptualizes it even if he is not characterized by it. And it is no secret that the reality of every science, among them kalām, consists of many conceptualizations and affirmations whose actual acquisition is sought through the method of reasoning and inference, so there is need for something that provides their conceptualization in an overall form that equals them, safeguarding the inquiry and reasoning from falling short of what is (required) and occupying oneself with what is not from them. That is the meaning of the definition of a science. And indeed its omission is frequent, especially in the religious and literary sciences, due to the prevalence of compiling sciences with their issues and proofs, and interpreting the conceptualizations related to them, then acquiring them likewise through learning from a teacher or understanding from a book. And when this is established, we say: The rulings attributed to the Law are of two types: those related to action, called subsidiary and practical, and those related to belief, called fundamental and doctrinal.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage defines ʿilm al-kalām and explains the nature and purpose of a scientific definition. The author first cites the definition: 'Kalām is the knowledge of religious beliefs based on certain proofs.' He then elaborates by distinguishing between two modes of acquiring qualities in the soul: sometimes one actually possesses the quality (ittiṣāf), and sometimes one merely has a mental form of it (taṣawwur). For example, a generous person is characterized by generosity even without conceptualizing it, while a stingy person may conceptualize generosity without being generous. The reality of any science consists of many conceptualizations (taṣawwurāt) and affirmations (taṣdīqāt) that are sought through reasoning. Therefore, one needs an overall conceptualization that sums up the science, to keep inquiry focused and avoid digression. This is the function of a definition. The author notes that definitions are often omitted in religious and literary sciences because these sciences are typically compiled with their issues and proofs, and their concepts are learned through teaching or reading. He then transitions to classifying sharʿī rulings: those related to action (farʿiyya/ʿamaliyya) and those related to belief (aṣliyya/iʿtiqādiyya), setting the stage for the science of kalām as the study of the latter.

العقائد الدينية

تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author argues that a definition is needed to provide a general conceptualization that safeguards inquiry from missing what belongs to the science and engaging with what does not.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The definition of kalām is 'knowledge of religious beliefs based on certain proofs,' and such a definition serves to provide a general conceptualization that guides systematic inquiry.

?

The Classification of Sharʿī Rulings
Al-Sanusi classifies sharʿī rulings into practical and doctrinal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sharʿī rulings are divided into practical and doctrinal categories, and how this sets the subject of kalām.

Core Idea
Rulings of the Law are either about action (fiqh) or about belief (kalām).

After defining kalām and explaining the purpose of definitions, al-Sanusi turns to the rulings of the Law. He distinguishes between those concerning action, which belong to fiqh, and those concerning belief, which are the subject of kalām. This lesson examines that classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of rulings
Read the Arabic sentence classifying rulings. Note the two types: those related to action (ʿamal) called farʿiyya and ʿamaliyya, and those related to belief (iʿtiqād) called aṣliyya and iʿtiqādiyya.
Practical rulings are the subject of fiqh; doctrinal rulings are the subject of kalām.
2
Connect to the definition of kalām
Recall that kalām was defined as knowledge of religious beliefs. Now you see that it specifically concerns the doctrinal rulings. This sets the scope of the science.
