Is the Concept of Knowledge Self-Evident? A Dialectical Refutation
al-Taftazani discusses whether the conception of knowledge is self-evident and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that the conception (تصور) of knowledge is not self-evident, and the key distinction between the occurrence of knowledge and its conceptualization.

Core Idea
Knowledge of existence is self-evident, but the concept 'knowledge' is not.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid delves into a foundational question: is the idea of knowledge itself self-evident? Through a series of objections and refutations, al-Taftazani demonstrates that while everyone possesses self-evident knowledge of their own existence, the abstract conception of knowledge requires definition and is not immediately known. This lesson unpacks the dialectical reasoning and clarifies the critical distinction between the occurrence (حصول) of knowledge and its conceptualization (تصور).

Guided Path
1
Read the initial claim and objection
Start by reading the passage that states the claim that conception of knowledge is self-evident and the objections based on circularity and precedence.
Notice the phrase 'لزم الدور' (circularity would result).
2
Understand the refutation: distinction between conception and occurrence
Examine how al-Taftazani refutes the objection by distinguishing between conception (تصور) and occurrence (حصول). The conception of knowledge is through something else, so no circularity occurs.
The self-evident is the occurrence, not the conception.
3
Follow the second objection and response
Read the objection that 'occurrence in the soul is knowledge itself' and al-Taftazani's response about non-original existence and the example of the disbeliever.
The example of the disbeliever illustrates the distinction between attributing disbelief and conceiving it.
4
Analyze the final objection and conclusion
Consider the final objection about knowledge of the absolute and the response that only a partial conception is necessary.
The conclusion is that the conception of knowledge is not fully self-evident; it requires some conceptualization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
تعريف العلم
قال المبحث الأول : قيل تصوره ضروري لأنه حاصل(۱)، وغيره إنما يعلم به، فلو علم هو بغيره لزم الدور: ولأن علم كل أحد بوجوده بديهي(٢) وهو مسبوق بمطلق العلم فهو أولى بالبداهة ورد بالفرق بين تصور العلم وحصوله . فتصور العلم بتصور غيره، وتصور الغير بحصوله فلا دور والبديهي حصول العلم بوجوده وهذا لا يستدعي تصور العلم فضلا عن بداهته، فإن قيل الحصول في النفس هو العلم .
قلنا : لا مطلقاً بل بوجود غير متأصل، ومصداقه الاتصاف وعدمه كالكافر يتصف بالكفر ولا يتصوره. ويتصور الإيمان ولا يتصف به . فإن قيل : حصول العلم بتغير (۳) يستلزم إمكان العلم بأنه عالم ويقضي إلى العلم بالمقيد قبل العلم بالمطلق، وأيضاً العلم بأنه عالم بوجوده بديهي لا يفتقر إلى نظر أصلا (٤) وجب المطلوب .
قلنا : لوسلم فاللازم التصور بوجه ما (٥)
(1) يقول صاحب أشرف المقاصد : لا يحتاج فيه إلى معرف بل يحصل بالالتفات إليه أو بسماع لفظه كتصور الإنسان وجود نفسه ؛ وتصوره شخص اليد والرجل عند سماع لفظهما .
(۲) حتى البله والصبيان ومن لا يتأتى منه النظر حتى لو تشكك أحد في وجود نفسه . خرج عن طور مطلق التمييز.
(۳) في (ب) بالمعنى .
(٤) سقط من (ب) كلمة أصلاً .
(٥) زيد في (ب) (كلمة) لتعريفات العلم).
The first topic: Definition of knowledge. He said in the first topic: It is said that its conception is necessary because it is [already] obtained, and other things are known through it; if it were known through something else, circularity would result. And because every person's knowledge of his own existence is self-evident, and it is preceded by absolute knowledge, so it is more worthy of being self-evident. It is refuted by the distinction between the conception of knowledge and its actual occurrence. For the conception of knowledge is through the conception of something else, and the conception of something else is through its occurrence, so there is no circularity. The self-evident is the occurrence of knowledge of one's existence, and this does not necessitate the conception of knowledge, let alone its self-evidence. If it is said: The occurrence in the soul is knowledge. We say: Not absolutely, but by a non-original existence, and its instantiation is attribution and its absence, like the disbeliever who is attributed with disbelief but does not conceive it, and conceives faith but is not attributed with it. If it is said: The occurrence of knowledge by [؟] entails the possibility of knowledge that he is knowing, and leads to knowledge of the qualified before knowledge of the absolute; also, knowledge that he is knowing by his existence is self-evident, not requiring any reasoning, so the desired follows. We say: Even if conceded, the necessary is conception in some respect. (1) The author of Ashraf al-Maqasid says: It does not need a definition but is obtained by attending to it or hearing its word, like the conception of man of his own existence, and his conception of the individual hand and foot when hearing their words. (2) Even the mentally deficient and children and those who cannot reason—even if one doubts his own existence, he leaves the realm of absolute discrimination. (3) In (B): by meaning. (4) Dropped from (B) the word 'originally'. (5) Added in (B): (word) 'to definitions of knowledge'.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on whether the conception (تصور) of knowledge (العلم) is self-evident (ضروري) or requires definition. It presents an objection to the claim of self-evidence and provides responses.

The argument begins: It is claimed that the conception of knowledge is necessary (self-evident) because knowledge is already obtained (حاصل) in the mind, and all other things are known through it. If knowledge itself were known through something else, there would be circularity (دور). Additionally, every person's knowledge of his own existence is self-evident (بديهي), and this knowledge is preceded by absolute knowledge (مطلق العلم), so absolute knowledge is even more worthy of being self-evident. This objection is refuted by distinguishing between the conception of knowledge (تصور العلم) and its actual occurrence (حصوله). The conception of knowledge is through the conception of something else, and the conception of that something else is through its occurrence in the mind. Thus there is no circularity. The self-evident is the actual occurrence of knowledge of one's existence, not the conception of knowledge itself. So the self-evidence of the occurrence does not imply self-evidence of the conception.

Then a further objection: It is said that the occurrence (الحصول) in the soul is knowledge itself. The response: Not absolutely; rather, it is a non-original existence (وجود غير متأصل). Its instantiation (مصداق) is the attribution (الاتصاف) or its absence, like the disbeliever who is characterized by disbelief but does not conceive it, and conceives faith but is not characterized by it. So occurrence and conception are distinct.

Another objection: The occurrence of knowledge (perhaps through 'meaning' or 'change'—note the variant in manuscript B) entails the possibility of knowing that one is knowing, leading to knowledge of the qualified before the absolute. Also, the knowledge that one is knowing by his existence is self-evident without reasoning, so the desired conclusion (that conception is self-evident) is established. The response: Even if conceded, the necessary conclusion is only a conception in some respect (تصور بوجه ما), not the full self-evident conception.

The footnotes provide additional explanations and manuscript variants: (1) From Ashraf al-Maqasid, stating that knowledge does not need definition but is obtained by attention or hearing the word, like conception of one's own existence or of hand and foot. (2) Even the mentally deficient and children have self-evident knowledge of their existence. (3) Variant in manuscript B: 'by meaning' instead of 'by change'. (4) The word 'originally' missing in B. (5) In B, an addition: 'a word for definitions of knowledge'.

Key Terms
العلم
التصور
البديهي
الدور
الحصول
الوجود غير المتأصل
الاتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because knowledge of existence is self-evident, the concept of knowledge itself is also self-evident.
Correction: The passage shows that the occurrence of knowledge (knowing that one exists) is self-evident, but the abstract concept of knowledge (تصور العلم) is not self-evident; it requires definition through other concepts.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given why the conception of knowledge cannot be self-evident?
A) Because it is known through something else, leading to circularity.
B) Because it is preceded by absolute knowledge, which is more worthy of self-evidence.
C) Because the conception of knowledge is through the conception of something else, and the conception of something else is through its occurrence, so no circularity occurs and the self-evident is the occurrence, not the conception.
D) Because all people, even the mentally deficient, know their own existence.

Key Takeaway
The conception of knowledge is not self-evident; it is a conceptualization that depends on other concepts, unlike the self-evident occurrence of knowledge of one's own existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you merely 'possess' knowledge of your own faith (like the disbeliever possesses disbelief without conceiving it) without truly conceiving its realities? What concrete step will you take today to move from attribution to genuine conception of one Islamic concept you thought you knew?
?
The passage argues that the self-evident occurrence of your own existence does not guarantee a self-evident conception of knowledge. Is your certainty in Allah's existence based on self-evident occurrence or on a conceived proof? If it is merely occurrence, how will you seek to conceive it more deeply?
?
You claim to know that 'Allah is the Creator,' but does that knowledge remain a mere occurrence (a fact you acknowledge) or have you actually conceived its implications? What will you change in your daily life to ensure your conceptions of divine attributes are actively attained, not passively inherited?

The Rebuttal of the Self-Evidence of Knowledge's Conception: Distinguishing Conception from Occurrence
The author presents and rebuts the argument that the conception of knowledge is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the conception of knowledge and its occurrence, and how this distinction resolves the alleged circularity in defining knowledge.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge avoids circularity by separating its conception from its occurrence.

Theologians debate whether the concept of knowledge is self-evident or requires definition. Some argue it must be self-evident to avoid circularity. In this passage, the author presents their argument and then rebuts it by drawing a crucial distinction between conceiving knowledge and experiencing its occurrence. This distinction is key to understanding how knowledge can be defined without circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim for self-evidence
Read the passage's opening where it states the argument: knowledge's conception is necessary because it is already obtained, and all else is known through it. Note the circularity objection if it were not self-evident.
2
Identify the rebuttal distinction
The author rebuts by distinguishing between 'conception of knowledge' (تصور العلم) and 'occurrence of knowledge' (حصول العلم). See how this distinction avoids circularity.
Conception is through something else, occurrence is the actual presence in the soul.
3
Examine the examples and final concession
Review the examples of the disbeliever and the final objection about knowledge of existence. Understand how the author concedes that some conception is necessary but not full self-evidence.
The final line concedes 'conception in some sense'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
تعريف العلم
قال المبحث الأول : قيل تصوره ضروري لأنه حاصل، وغيره إنما يعلم به، فلو علم هو بغيره لزم الدور: ولأن علم كل أحد بوجوده بديهي وهو مسبوق بمطلق العلم فهو أولى بالبداهة ورد بالفرق بين تصور العلم وحصوله . فتصور العلم بتصور غيره، وتصور الغير بحصوله فلا دور والبديهي حصول العلم بوجوده وهذا لا يستدعي تصور العلم فضلا عن بداهته، فإن قيل الحصول في النفس هو العلم .
قلنا : لا مطلقاً بل بوجود غير متأصل، ومصداقه الاتصاف وعدمه كالكافر
يتصف بالكفر ولا يتصوره. ويتصور الإيمان ولا يتصف به . فإن قيل : حصول العلم بتغير يستلزم إمكان العلم بأنه عالم ويقضي إلى العلم بالمقيد قبل العلم بالمطلق، وأيضاً العلم بأنه عالم بوجوده بديهي لا يفتقر إلى نظر أصلا وجب المطلوب .
قلنا : لوسلم فاللازم التصور بوجه ما
The first chapter: The definition of knowledge. The first chapter said: It was said that its conception is necessary because it is obtained, and other than it is only known through it; so if it were known through other than it, circularity would follow. And because everyone's knowledge of his own existence is self-evident, and it is preceded by absolute knowledge, so it is more deserving of self-evidence. It was rebutted by the distinction between the conception of knowledge and its occurrence. For the conception of knowledge is through conceiving something else, and the conception of that other is through its occurrence, so there is no circularity. The self-evident is the occurrence of knowledge of one's existence, and this does not require the conception of knowledge, let alone its self-evidence. If it is said: occurrence in the soul is knowledge. We say: Not absolutely, but by a non-substantial existence, and its meaning is qualification and its absence, like the disbeliever who is qualified by disbelief but does not conceive it, and conceives faith but is not qualified by it. If it is said: The occurrence of knowledge through change necessitates the possibility of knowledge that he is knowing, and leads to knowledge of the qualified before knowledge of the absolute; also, knowledge that he is knowing in his existence is self-evident, not requiring any reflection, so the required is necessitated. We say: Even if conceded, the necessary is conception in some sense.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a debate on whether the conception (تصور) of knowledge (العلم) is self-evident (ضروري). The author first presents the claim that it is self-evident because it is inherently obtained (حاصل) and other things are known through it; if it were known through something else, circularity (دور) would result. Also, everyone's knowledge of their own existence is self-evident, and that is preceded by absolute knowledge, so absolute knowledge is even more self-evident. The rebuttal introduces the key distinction between conception (تصور العلم) and occurrence (حصول العلم). The conception of knowledge occurs through conceiving something else, and conceiving that other depends on the occurrence of knowledge, not its conception, so no circularity. The self-evident is the occurrence of knowledge of one's own existence, which does not require conceiving knowledge itself. A further objection is raised: if occurrence in the soul is knowledge itself, the author responds that it is not absolute but a non-substantial existence, exemplified by attribution and its absence, like a disbeliever who is described by disbelief without conceiving it, and conceives faith without being described by it. Another objection argues that occurrence through change implies knowledge of being knowing, leading to knowledge of the qualified before the absolute, and that knowledge of existence is self-evident, so the required follows. The author concedes that even if so, the necessary is conception in some sense.

حصول العلم
بديهي

العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that the conception of knowledge is self-evident because we all have knowledge, and any attempt to define it leads to circularity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that self-evidence applies to the occurrence of knowledge (e.g., knowing one's own existence), not to its conception. The conception of knowledge is defined through something else, avoiding circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what crucial distinction avoids circularity in defining knowledge?
A) Between necessary and acquired knowledge
B) Between conception and occurrence of knowledge
C) Between universal and particular knowledge
D) Between self-evident and inferred knowledge

Key Takeaway
The conception of knowledge is not self-evident; it is defined through its occurrence, avoiding circularity.

Al-Razi's Proofs for Self-Evidence of Knowledge
Al-Razi gives two arguments for the self-evidence of the conception of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's two arguments for why the conception of knowledge is self-evident and cannot be acquired.

Core Idea
Al-Razi uses circularity and self-knowledge to argue knowledge's conception is innate.

Imam al-Razi presents two intriguing proofs that the very concept of knowledge is self-evident (بديهي) and cannot be acquired through definition. The first relies on the impossibility of circularity; the second on the self-evidence of our own existence. Let's examine these arguments closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Carefully read the first argument: knowledge is known by intuition and cannot be acquired because acquisition would require circularity.
Notice how he uses the term 'necessity' (الضرورة) as the only way.
2
Read the second proof
Read the second argument: everyone's knowledge of their own existence is self-evident, and absolute knowledge is a component, so it too is self-evident.
This argument relies on the composition of specific knowledge from absolute and particular.
3
Identify the common theme
Both proofs aim to show that the conception of knowledge is self-evident and cannot be acquired.
The first uses circularity, the second uses self-evidence of self-knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب الإمام الرازي إلى أن تصور العلم بديهي لوجهين :
الأول أنه معلوم ، يمتنع اكتسابه أما المعلومية فبحكم الوجدان وأما امتناع الاكتساب فلأنه إنما يكون بغيره معلوماً ضرورة امتناع اكتساب الشيء بنفسه أو ه مجهولاً، والغير إنما يعلم بالعلم، فلو علم العلم بالغير لزم الدور، فتعين طريق الضرورة وهو المطلوب .
الثاني : أن علم كل أحد بوجوده بديهي أي حاصل من غير نظر وكسب هذا علم خاص مسبوق لمطلق العلم لتركبه منه ومن الخصوصية، والسابق على البديهي بديهي، بل أولى بالبداهة، فمطلق العلم بديهي وهو المطلوب. وأجيب عن الوجهين بأن مبناهما على عدم التفرقة بين تصور العلم
وحصوله .
أما الأول : فلان تصور العلم على تقدير اكتسابه يتوقف على تصور غيره، وتصور الغير لا يتوقف على تصوره ليلزم الدور، بل على حصوله بناء على امتناع حصول المقيد بدون المطلق، حتى لو لم يقل بوجود الكلي في ضمن الجزئيات لم يتوقف على حصوله أيضاً، وعبارة المواقف : إن الذي نحاول أن نعلمه بغير العلم تصور حقيقة العلم، وقد تسامح حيث حاول العلم بتصور الحقيقة والأحسن ما في شرح المختصر أن الذي يراد حصوله بالغير إنما هو تصور حقيقة العلم ، إلا أنه تسامح فيه أيضاً حيث قال : لا يمكن تعريفه .
Imam al-Razi held that the conception of knowledge is self-evident for two reasons: First, it is known; its acquisition is impossible. As for its being known, it is by inner intuition; as for the impossibility of acquisition, it is because it can only be known through something else, due to the impossibility of acquiring a thing by itself while it is unknown; and the other is only known through knowledge; so if knowledge were known through the other, circularity would follow, so the way of necessity is determined, which is the required. Second, everyone's knowledge of his own existence is self-evident, i.e., obtained without reflection and acquisition; this is a specific knowledge preceded by absolute knowledge, because it is composed of it and a particularity, and what precedes the self-evident is self-evident, indeed more deserving of self-evidence; so absolute knowledge is self-evident, which is the required. And it was answered concerning the two proofs that they are based on the failure to distinguish between the conception of knowledge and its occurrence. As for the first: Because the conception of knowledge, on the supposition of its acquisition, depends on conceiving something else; and the conception of that other does not depend on its conception so that circularity would occur, but rather on its occurrence, based on the impossibility of the occurrence of the qualified without the absolute; even if one does not hold to the existence of the universal within particulars, it does not depend on its occurrence either. And the expression of al-Mawaqif: 'What we are trying to know through other than knowledge is the conception of the reality of knowledge,' and he was lenient when he tried to know knowledge through the conception of reality. And the better is what is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: 'What is intended to be obtained through other is only the conception of the reality of knowledge,' except that he was also lenient in it when he said: 'It cannot be defined.'
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's position that the conception (تصور) of knowledge is self-evident (بديهي), with two proofs. Proof one: Knowledge is known by inner intuition (الوجدان) and cannot be acquired because acquisition would require being known by something else, but that other is known only through knowledge, leading to circularity (دور), so necessity (الضرورة) is the only way. Proof two: Everyone's knowledge of their own existence is self-evident and obtained without reflection; this is a specific knowledge composed of absolute knowledge and a particularity, and what precedes a self-evident is even more self-evident, therefore absolute knowledge is self-evident. The response to both proofs is that they fail because they do not distinguish between the conception of knowledge and its occurrence. For the first proof: Even if conception is acquired, it depends on conceiving something else, and conceiving that other depends on the occurrence of knowledge, not its conception, so no circularity. This is based on the principle that the qualified cannot occur without the absolute; even if one denies universals in particulars, it still does not depend on occurrence. The passage then quotes al-Mawaqif and Sharh al-Mukhtasar to clarify that what is sought to be known through other is the conception of the reality of knowledge, though there is some looseness in expression.

Key Terms
تصور العلم
بديهي
دور
الوجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second proof based on?
A) Circularity
B) Self-evidence of self-knowledge
C) Impossibility of acquisition
D) Inner intuition

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses logical necessity and the self-evidence of self-knowledge to argue that knowledge's conception is self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on the concept of knowledge every day, but have you ever considered that your very understanding of what it means to know something is self-evident? How does that shape the certainty you have in your beliefs? Will you now test your assumptions against this foundational truth?
?
Al-Razi says your knowledge of your own existence is self-evident. But do you live as if you truly know yourself? What are you actively doing to know your own soul, and are you using the self-evidence of that knowledge as a starting point to seek deeper truths?
?
The circularity argument shows that some things must be taken as given. In your spiritual life, do you resist accepting self-evident truths about Allah and His attributes because you want a proof for everything? What would change if you embraced the self-evident as al-Razi suggests?

Refutation: Distinguishing Conception from Occurrence
The refutation distinguishes conception and occurrence to answer al-Razi's proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key refutation of al-Razi's proofs based on the distinction between the conception of knowledge and its occurrence.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's arguments fail because they conflate the conception of knowledge with its occurrence.

The response to al-Razi's arguments pinpoints a crucial distinction: between the conception (تصور) of knowledge and its occurrence (حصول). This distinction breaks the supposed circularity and shows that knowledge can be acquired through definition. Let's see how the refutation applies to each proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the refutation's basis
The response says the proofs fail because they confuse conception with occurrence. Grasp this distinction.
Conception is the idea of something; occurrence is its actual existence in the mind.
2
Apply to the first proof
Even if the conception of knowledge is acquired, it depends on another's conception; but that other's conception depends on the occurrence of knowledge, not its conception, so no circularity.
This uses the principle that the qualified depends on the absolute in occurrence, not conception.
3
Note the clarification from other sources
The text quotes al-Mawaqif and Sharh al-Mukhtasar to clarify that what is sought is the conception of the reality of knowledge, and that definition may still be possible.
There is some leniency in expressions; the better view is that definition can be given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب الإمام الرازي إلى أن تصور العلم بديهي لوجهين :
الأول أنه معلوم ، يمتنع اكتسابه أما المعلومية فبحكم الوجدان وأما امتناع الاكتساب فلأنه إنما يكون بغيره معلوماً ضرورة امتناع اكتساب الشيء بنفسه أو ه مجهولاً، والغير إنما يعلم بالعلم، فلو علم العلم بالغير لزم الدور، فتعين طريق الضرورة وهو المطلوب .
الثاني : أن علم كل أحد بوجوده بديهي أي حاصل من غير نظر وكسب هذا علم خاص مسبوق لمطلق العلم لتركبه منه ومن الخصوصية، والسابق على البديهي بديهي، بل أولى بالبداهة، فمطلق العلم بديهي وهو المطلوب. وأجيب عن الوجهين بأن مبناهما على عدم التفرقة بين تصور العلم
وحصوله .
أما الأول : فلان تصور العلم على تقدير اكتسابه يتوقف على تصور غيره، وتصور الغير لا يتوقف على تصوره ليلزم الدور، بل على حصوله بناء على امتناع حصول المقيد بدون المطلق، حتى لو لم يقل بوجود الكلي في ضمن الجزئيات لم يتوقف على حصوله أيضاً، وعبارة المواقف : إن الذي نحاول أن نعلمه بغير العلم تصور حقيقة العلم، وقد تسامح حيث حاول العلم بتصور الحقيقة والأحسن ما في شرح المختصر أن الذي يراد حصوله بالغير إنما هو تصور حقيقة العلم ، إلا أنه تسامح فيه أيضاً حيث قال : لا يمكن تعريفه .
Imam al-Razi held that the conception of knowledge is self-evident for two reasons: First, it is known; its acquisition is impossible. As for its being known, it is by inner intuition; as for the impossibility of acquisition, it is because it can only be known through something else, due to the impossibility of acquiring a thing by itself while it is unknown; and the other is only known through knowledge; so if knowledge were known through the other, circularity would follow, so the way of necessity is determined, which is the required. Second, everyone's knowledge of his own existence is self-evident, i.e., obtained without reflection and acquisition; this is a specific knowledge preceded by absolute knowledge, because it is composed of it and a particularity, and what precedes the self-evident is self-evident, indeed more deserving of self-evidence; so absolute knowledge is self-evident, which is the required. And it was answered concerning the two proofs that they are based on the failure to distinguish between the conception of knowledge and its occurrence. As for the first: Because the conception of knowledge, on the supposition of its acquisition, depends on conceiving something else; and the conception of that other does not depend on its conception so that circularity would occur, but rather on its occurrence, based on the impossibility of the occurrence of the qualified without the absolute; even if one does not hold to the existence of the universal within particulars, it does not depend on its occurrence either. And the expression of al-Mawaqif: 'What we are trying to know through other than knowledge is the conception of the reality of knowledge,' and he was lenient when he tried to know knowledge through the conception of reality. And the better is what is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: 'What is intended to be obtained through other is only the conception of the reality of knowledge,' except that he was also lenient in it when he said: 'It cannot be defined.'
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's position that the conception (تصور) of knowledge is self-evident (بديهي), with two proofs. Proof one: Knowledge is known by inner intuition (الوجدان) and cannot be acquired because acquisition would require being known by something else, but that other is known only through knowledge, leading to circularity (دور), so necessity (الضرورة) is the only way. Proof two: Everyone's knowledge of their own existence is self-evident and obtained without reflection; this is a specific knowledge composed of absolute knowledge and a particularity, and what precedes a self-evident is even more self-evident, therefore absolute knowledge is self-evident. The response to both proofs is that they fail because they do not distinguish between the conception of knowledge and its occurrence. For the first proof: Even if conception is acquired, it depends on conceiving something else, and conceiving that other depends on the occurrence of knowledge, not its conception, so no circularity. This is based on the principle that the qualified cannot occur without the absolute; even if one denies universals in particulars, it still does not depend on occurrence. The passage then quotes al-Mawaqif and Sharh al-Mukhtasar to clarify that what is sought to be known through other is the conception of the reality of knowledge, though there is some looseness in expression.

Key Terms
تصور
حصول
دور
المطلق
المقيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that refutes al-Razi's arguments?
A) Between conception and occurrence
B) Between absolute and specific knowledge
C) Between intuition and acquisition
D) Between circularity and necessity

Key Takeaway
The distinction between conception and occurrence resolves the circularity and shows that knowledge's conception can be defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to define foundational concepts in your faith (like knowledge, belief, or truth), do you ever feel trapped in circular reasoning? How does distinguishing 'conception' from 'occurrence' offer a way out of that mental prison?
?
The refutation shows that al-Razi's error was conflating two different aspects of reality. In your own spiritual journey, are you confusing the mental image you have of a concept (like tawheed) with its actual reality? What specific step can you take today to move from conception to occurrence?
?
The passage emphasizes that the occurrence of the qualified requires the occurrence of the absolute. Consider your own worship: are you treating acts of obedience as 'qualified' without anchoring them in the 'absolute' reality of Allah? How would this distinction transform the way you approach your prayers?

The Deficiency of Definitions of Knowledge and al-Ghazali's View
The author discusses the deficiency of common definitions of knowledge and cites al-Ghazali's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why most definitions of knowledge are considered defective and the two perspectives on the difficulty of defining it.

Core Idea
Most definitions of knowledge fail—either because knowledge is too hidden or too clear.

In this passage, the author examines common definitions of knowledge and points out their shortcomings. He then presents two contrasting explanations for this difficulty: one from al-Ghazali, who attributes it to the hiddenness of knowledge, and another from the investigators (al-muhaqqiqun), who see the problem as its clarity. We will explore these views and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'most definitions are defective' sets the tone.
2
Identify the two explanations
The passage gives two reasons for defective definitions: hiddenness (khafa') according to al-Ghazali, and clarity (wuduh) according to the investigators. Find these in the text.
These are contrasting views on why definitions fail.
3
Examine the example definitions
The passage lists examples: 'knowledge of the known as it is', 'affirmation of the known as it is', 'belief in the thing as it is'. Notice these are all considered defective.
These are common definitions from different schools.
4
Analyze al-Ghazali's argument
Al-Ghazali distinguishes between true definition (had haqiqi with genus and differentia) and useful definition via division and example. He says the former is difficult, not the latter.
The difficulty is in providing a precise logical definition.
5
Note the critique and conclusion
Al-Ghazali's view is criticized by al-Juwayni. The passage ends by reiterating the investigators' view that the difficulty is due to clarity.
This shows scholarly disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ثم أكثر تعريفات العلم مدخولة قيل لخفائه، والمحققون لوضوحه. ولا نزاع في اشتراك لفظه كقولهم: معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به. قوله : أكثر تعريفات العلم مدخولة أي فيها دخل وهو الفساد والخلل وهذا أمر متفق عليه في أكثر تعريفات العلم وذلك كقولهم : هو معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به وقولهم هو إثبات المعلوم على ما هو به. وقولهم : هو اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به قوله :لخفائه قاله حجة الإسلام الغزالي رضي الله عنه في كتابه (المستصفى) حيث ذكر فيه أنه يعسر تحديده على الوجه الحقيقي بعبارة محررة جامعة للجنس والفصل قال : فإن ذلك متعسر في أكثر الأشياء بل أكثر المدركات الحسية : فكيف في الإدراكات ..؟ وهذا الكلام من حجة الإسلام يفهم أن العسير هو تفهيمه بالحد الحقيقي كما أشار إلى ذلك بقوله يعسر تحديده على الوجه الحقيقي بعبارة محررة للجنس والفصل. وليس العسير مطلق تعريفه المفيد لامتيازه وتفهم حقيقته في الجملة فإن ذلك متيسر كما يفيده التقسيم والمثال . والذي يقوله الغزالي عليه إمام الحرمين الجويني. وقوله : والمحققون لوضوحه، وشدة الوضوح :
He said: Then most definitions of knowledge are defective; it is said because of its hiddenness, and the investigators [say] because of its clarity. And there is no dispute about the commonness of its word, as they say: it is knowledge (ma'rifa) of the known as it is. His saying: 'most definitions of knowledge are defective' means there is in them a defect (dakhal), which is corruption and disorder, and this is agreed upon in most definitions of knowledge, such as their saying: it is knowledge of the known as it is, and their saying: it is affirmation (ithbat) of the known as it is, and their saying: it is belief (i'tiqad) of the thing as it is. His saying: 'because of its hiddenness' (li-khafa'ihi) was said by Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him, in his book al-Mustasfa, where he mentioned that it is difficult to define it in the true sense (al-wajh al-haqiqi) with an expression that is precise, comprising genus and differentia. He said: indeed that is difficult in most things, even most sense-perceptibles, so how about intellectual perceptions? And this speech of Hujjat al-Islam indicates that the difficulty is in explaining it by a true definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi), as he indicated by his saying 'it is difficult to define it in the true sense with an expression precise as to genus and differentia.' And the difficulty is not the absolute definition that is useful for distinguishing it and understanding its reality in general, for that is easy as indicated by division (taqsim) and example (mithal). And what al-Ghazali says is objected to by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. And his saying: 'the investigators [say] because of its clarity' and intensity of clarity.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage discusses the definitions of knowledge (al-ilm). The author states that most definitions are defective (madkhula), with two opinions: some say it is because knowledge is hidden (khafa'), while the investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) say it is because of its clarity (wuduh). There is agreement that the word 'knowledge' is used commonly for various definitions, such as 'knowledge of the known as it is', 'affirmation of the known as it is', 'belief in the thing as it is'. The author elaborates, quoting al-Ghazali from al-Mustasfa, who argued that true definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi) with genus and differentia is difficult, even for sense objects, let alone intellectual concepts. However, the difficulty is not in giving a useful definition that distinguishes it, which can be done through division and example. Al-Ghazali's view is criticized by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. The passage concludes by noting that the investigators say the difficulty is due to clarity, not hiddenness.

مدخولة (defective)
خفاء (hiddenness)
وضوح (clarity)
حد حقيقي (true definition)

تقسيم ومثال (division and example)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the difficulty in defining knowledge means it cannot be defined at all.
Correction: Al-Ghazali's point is about the difficulty of a true definition with genus and differentia, not about providing a useful definition through division and example.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali as cited in the passage, why is it difficult to define knowledge?
A) Because knowledge is completely hidden and incomprehensible.
B) Because of the difficulty of providing a true definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi) with genus and differentia.
C) Because there is no agreed-upon word for knowledge.
D) Because knowledge is a simple concept that cannot be analyzed.

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that while definitions of knowledge are often defective, the difficulty lies in providing a true definition with genus and differentia, not in distinguishing it through division and example.

Defining Knowledge: al-Ghazali's Method of Division and Example
Al-Ghazali's method of defining knowledge via division and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ghazali avoided a real definition of knowledge and instead used division and example to clarify its meaning.

Core Idea
Real definitions fail for subtle concepts; division and example succeed.

Al-Ghazali recognized that defining knowledge with a precise genus-differentia formula is nearly impossible. Instead, he proposed two powerful tools: dividing knowledge from related mental states, and likening it to sensory perception. This lesson explores that method and why it works.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definitions and note their defects
Start by reading the list of common definitions for knowledge. Observe that each has flaws, as the author says 'The aspects of defect are apparent.'
These definitions include 'apprehension of the known', 'affirmation of the known', etc.
2
Understand al-Ghazali's reservation about real definitions
Al-Ghazali argues that a real definition (with genus and differentia) is extremely difficult, even for sensory objects, let alone abstract concepts like knowledge.
He says 'perhaps its definition in the true sense... is difficult'.
3
Examine the division: how knowledge is distinguished
The division method separates knowledge from doubt, conjecture, ignorance, and blind belief. Knowledge is certain, matches reality, and disappears when its object is negated.
Notice that belief can persist even after its object changes, unlike knowledge.
4
Study the example: knowledge as intellectual vision
Al-Ghazali likens intellect to a mirror that receives images of intelligibles, just as sight receives images of visible objects. Knowledge is the impression of these images in the intellect.
This is an analogy, not a literal definition.
5
Reflect on the author's clarification
The author concludes that al-Ghazali's intention was not a real definition but to clarify meaning through division and example, contrary to what some understood.
The example is not a specific instance like 'one is half of two'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو إدراك المعلوم على ما هو به أو إثبات المعلوم على ما هو به أو اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به . أو ما يعلم به الشيء أو ما يوجب كون من قام به عالماً، إلى غير ذلك ووجوه الخلل ظاهرة، إلا أن ذلك عند الإمام حجة الإسلام لخفاء معنى العلم وعسر تحديده قال في المستصفى ربما يعسر تحديده على الوجه الحقيقي بعبارة محررة جامعة للجنس والفصل فإن ذلك متعسر في أكثر الأشياء بل أكثر المدركات الحسية فكيف في الادراكات . . ؟ وإنما يبين معناه بتقسيم ومثال ، أما التقسيم فهو أن تميزه الاعتقادات ولا خفاء في غيره عن الشك والظن بالجزم وعن عما يلتبس به وهي الجهل بالمطابقة فلم يبق إلا اعتقاد المقلد ويتميز عنه بأن الاعتقاد قد يبقى مع تغير متعلقه كما إذا اعتقد كون زيد في الدار ثم خرج زيد، واعتقاد بحاله بخلاف العلم فإنه يتغير المعلوم ولا يتبقى عند اعتقاد انتفاء المتعلق لأنه كشف وانحلال في العقيدة والاعتقاد عقد على القلب ولهذا يزول بتشكيك المشكك بخلاف العلم . وأما المثال : فهو أن إدراك البصيرة شبه بإدراك الباصرة، فكما أنه لا معنى للابصار إلا انطباع صور المبصر أي مثاله المطابق في القوة الباصرة كانطباع الصورة في المرآة كذلك العقل بمنزلة مرآة تنطبع فيها صور المعقولات أي حقائقها وماهياتها على ما هي عليها العلم عبارة عن أخذ العقل صور المعقولات في نفسه وانطباعها حصولها فيه فالتقسيم المذكور يقطع العلم عن مظان الاشتباه هذا المثال يفهمك حقيقة العلم هذا كلامه فظهر أنه يريد عسر تحديده بالحد الحقيقي لا ما يفيد امتيازه وتفهيم حقيقته، وأن ذلك ليس ببعيد، وأنه لا يريد بالمثال جزئياً من جزئياته كاعتقادنا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين، على ما فهمه البعض .
Or: apprehension of the known as it is; or: affirmation of the known as it is; or: belief of a thing as it is; or that by which a thing is known; or that which necessitates one who possesses it being knowledgeable, etc. The aspects of defect are apparent, except that according to the Imam Hujjat al-Islam (al-Ghazali) this is due to the hiddenness of the meaning of knowledge and the difficulty of defining it. He said in al-Mustasfa: 'Perhaps its definition in the true sense with a precise expression including genus and differentia is difficult, for that is difficult in most things, indeed in most sensory perceptions, so how about perceptions?' Rather, its meaning is clarified by a division and an example. As for the division: it is that you distinguish it from beliefs, and there is no obscurity in [distinguishing] it from doubt and conjecture by certainty, and from what is confusable with it, which is ignorance [combined with] correspondence, so nothing remains except the belief of the muqallid (blind follower). It is distinguished from that because belief may remain with change of its object, as when one believes that Zayd is in the house, then Zayd leaves, while the belief remains, unlike knowledge, for the known changes and it does not remain when the non-existence of the object is believed, because it is disclosure and dissolution in certainty, while belief is a knot on the heart; therefore it is removed by the doubt of the sceptic, unlike knowledge. As for the example: it is that insight is similar to sight; just as there is no meaning to sight except the impression of images of the seen — i.e., their corresponding likeness — in the visual faculty, like the impression of an image in a mirror, likewise the intellect is like a mirror in which images of intelligibles — i.e., their realities and quiddities as they are — are impressed. Knowledge is an expression of the intellect's taking images of intelligibles into itself and their impression, i.e., their attainment in it. So the mentioned division cuts off knowledge from the loci of confusion. This example makes you understand the reality of knowledge. This is his speech. So it became clear that he intends the difficulty of defining it by a real definition, not [something] that yields its distinctiveness and makes its reality understood, and that that is not far-fetched, and that he does not intend by the example a particular instance of its instances, like our belief that one is half of two, as some understood.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage discusses the difficulty of defining knowledge ('ilm) and presents al-Ghazali's method of clarifying its meaning through division (taqsīm) and example (mithāl). The author first lists several common definitions of knowledge—such as apprehension of the known, affirmation, belief, etc.—and notes that each has defects. He then cites al-Ghazali, who argues that a real definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī) is nearly impossible because knowledge is too subtle. Instead, al-Ghazali proposes two complementary methods. The division distinguishes knowledge from other cognitive states: it is certain (jazm), unlike doubt (shakk) and conjecture (ẓann); it corresponds to reality (muṭābaqa), unlike ignorance; and it differs from the belief of a muqallid (blind follower) because knowledge disappears when its object is negated, whereas belief can persist. The example likens intellectual apprehension to visual perception: as sight receives images of visible objects, so the intellect receives images of intelligibles. The author then clarifies that al-Ghazali's intention was not to give a strict definition but to make the meaning clear through these aids, and that the example is not meant as a particular instance like 'one is half of two'.

تعريف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ghazali's example was meant as a real definition rather than an illustrative analogy.
Correction: Al-Ghazali's example is an analogy to convey the reality of knowledge, not a strict definition. The author explicitly states that al-Ghazali did not intend a real definition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider al-Ghazali's approach to defining knowledge via division and example valid despite the defects of other definitions?

B) Because the division and example clarify the meaning without claiming a real definition.
C) Because al-Ghazali rejects the need for any definition.
D) Because the division is only about distinguishing knowledge from ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's method of division and example shows that clarifying a concept's meaning can be more effective than attempting an impossible real definition.

If that opinion were proven false, would it vanish instantly, or would you cling to it? What does your answer say about whether you truly know them or merely believe? Al-Razi on the Self-Evident Nature of Knowledge
Al-Razi argues that definitions of knowledge are flawed because knowledge is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that knowledge cannot be defined because it is maximally evident, and how this differs from al-Ghazali's view of knowledge as hidden.

Core Idea
Knowledge is too obvious to define — its clarity is the very reason definitions fail.

Al-Razi challenges the very project of defining knowledge. While earlier thinkers like al-Ghazali thought knowledge was difficult to define because it was hidden, al-Razi turns this on its head: the problem is that knowledge is too obvious. In this lesson, we examine his provocative claim that all definitions of knowledge are inherently flawed because nothing is clearer than knowledge itself.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: علم, تعريف, ماهية, ظهور.
Al-Razi uses the word ظهور (obviousness) as the central reason.
2
Identify the core claim
Al-Razi says all definitions of knowledge are defective. Identify his reasoning: because its quiddity has reached a degree of obviousness that nothing is clearer.
This is a direct argument from self-evidence.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how this view contrasts with al-Ghazali's earlier position. The common misunderstanding is that both thought knowledge was hard to define for the same reason. Reflect on why clarity versus hiddenness leads to different conclusions.
This helps situate al-Razi within the kalam tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الرازي تعريفات العلم لا تخلو عن خلل لأن ماهيته قد بلغت في الظهور إلى حيث لا يمكن تعريفه بشيء أجلى منه، وإلى هذا ذهب كثير من المحققين، حتى قال بعضهم : وإن ما وقع فيه من الاختلاف إنما هو وضوحه لا لخفائه .
And Imam al-Razi said: Definitions of knowledge are not free from defect, because its quiddity has reached such a degree of obviousness that it cannot be defined by anything clearer than it. Many investigators held this view, to the point that some of them said: The disagreement that has occurred about it is only due to its clarity, not its hiddenness.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and many other investigators regarding the definition of knowledge. Al-Razi states that all definitions of 'ilm (knowledge) are flawed. The reason is that knowledge is extremely self-evident (ẓuhūr) — it is clearer than anything that could be used to define it. Since a definition aims to clarify something by reference to something more known, and knowledge is already maximally clear, any definition would be circular or less clear. The author adds that many investigators agreed, and some even said that the scholarly disagreement about the definition of knowledge is not due to its obscurity but precisely because of its excessive clarity — different formulations attempt to capture something that is too obvious to pin down. This view contrasts with al-Ghazali's earlier position that knowledge is difficult to define because it is hidden; here the difficulty is ascribed to its extreme obviousness.

Key Terms
علم
تعريف
ماهية
ظهور
وضوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Razi and al-Ghazali both thought knowledge is hard to define for the same reason.
Correction: Al-Ghazali cited hiddenness as the cause, while al-Razi cited extreme clarity. Al-Razi’s view is that knowledge is too obvious to be captured in a definition.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why are all definitions of knowledge defective?
A) Because knowledge is too hidden to be defined.
B) Because knowledge is so clear that nothing is clearer to define it.
C) Because no one has ever attempted a correct definition.
D) Because definitions only apply to physical objects.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that knowledge is too self-evident to be defined, and disagreement about it stems from its clarity rather than obscurity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Razi says disagreement about knowledge is due to its clarity, not its hiddenness. Where in your life have you turned a simple, clear obligation into a source of debate or confusion? Will you act on what is already clear to you before seeking further definition?

The Difficulty of Defining Knowledge and the Analogy of Vision
Author explains the difficulty of defining knowledge and gives an analogy from vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining knowledge is challenging and how an analogy of vision helps illustrate its nature without providing a real definition.

Core Idea
Knowledge is so clear that nothing can define it more clearly.

This passage tackles a fundamental issue in Islamic theology and philosophy: how to define knowledge. The author begins by distinguishing knowledge from ambiguous concepts and then introduces an analogy comparing intellectual perception to vision. This analogy, while helpful, is not a full definition. Finally, he cites Imam al-Razi, who points out that knowledge is so self-evident that any definition falls short. Understanding this sets the stage for why kalam proceeds with concepts that are intuitively grasped rather than strictly defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation without overthinking. Get a sense of the flow and the key claims: the analogy, the difficulty of defining, and the quote from al-Razi.
Notice the shift from the analogy to the quotation.
2
Identify the main argument
Identify the thesis: knowledge is difficult to define because it is too obvious. The author uses an analogy to show what knowledge is like, not to define it precisely.
3
Analyze the analogy
Examine how the author compares inner sight to outer sight. Both involve an impression of the object. For sight, it's the image in the eye; for knowledge, it's the forms of intelligibles in the intellect.
This is not a real definition but a clarifying example.
4
Consider the misconception
The author warns that some mistakenly took the example as referring to a particular instance of knowledge (like knowing one is half of two). Understand why that interpretation is wrong.
The example is meant to illustrate the process, not a specific case.
5
Reflect on al-Razi's statement
Read Imam al-Razi's claim that definitions of knowledge are flawed because its quiddity is so apparent. Reflect on how this paradox—extreme clarity causing difficulty—impacts theological discourse.
This is a key insight for kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشكك بخلاف العلم . وأما المثال : فهو أن إدراك البصيرة شبه بإدراك الباصرة، فكما أنه لا معنى للابصار إلا انطباع صور المبصر أي مثاله المطابق في القوة الباصرة كانطباع الصورة في المرآة كذلك العقل بمنزلة مرآة تنطبع فيها صور المعقولات أي حقائقها وماهياتها على ما هي عليها العلم عبارة عن أخذ العقل صور المعقولات في نفسه وانطباعها حصولها فيه فالتقسيم المذكور يقطع العلم عن مظان الاشتباه هذا المثال يفهمك حقيقة العلم هذا كلامه فظهر أنه يريد عسر تحديده بالحد الحقيقي لا ما يفيد امتيازه وتفهيم حقيقته، وأن ذلك ليس ببعيد، وأنه لا يريد بالمثال جزئياً من جزئياته كاعتقادنا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين، على ما فهمه البعض . وقال الإمام الرازي تعريفات العلم لا تخلو عن خلل لأن ماهيته قد بلغت في الظهور إلى حيث لا يمكن تعريفه بشيء أجلى منه ، وإلى هذا ذهب كثير من المحققين، حتى قال بعضهم : وإن ما وقع فيه من الاختلاف إنما هو وضوحه لا لخفائه .
The ambiguous is unlike knowledge. As for the example: it is that the perception of the inner sight is similar to the perception of the outer sight. For just as there is no meaning of sight except the impression of the images of the visible object, i.e., its matching likeness, in the visual power, like the impression of the form in a mirror, so likewise the intellect is like a mirror in which are impressed the images of intelligibles, i.e., their realities and quiddities as they are. Knowledge is an expression for the intellect's taking the images of intelligibles into itself and their being impressed [and] occurring in it. So the aforementioned division cuts off knowledge from the loci of confusion. This example makes you understand the reality of knowledge. This is his statement. So it became clear that he intends the difficulty of defining it by a real definition, not that which gives its distinction and understanding of its reality, and that that is not far, and that he does not intend by the example a particular instance among its instances, such as our belief that one is half of two, as some understood. And Imam al-Razi said: definitions of knowledge are not free from defect, because its quiddity has reached such a degree of obviousness that it cannot be defined by anything clearer than it. And many of the verifiers have gone to this, until some of them said: the disagreement that has occurred concerning it is only because of its clarity, not because of its hiddenness.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage discusses the difficulty of defining knowledge. The author begins by contrasting knowledge with ambiguous things. He then gives an analogy: inner perception (of the intellect) is like outer sight. Just as sight is the impression of the image of the seen object in the eye, so knowledge is the impression of the forms of intelligible objects in the intellect. The author clarifies that this example is not meant as a complete real definition but only to give an understanding. He then quotes Imam al-Razi, who states that definitions of knowledge are always somewhat flawed because knowledge is so manifest that nothing clearer can be used to define it. Many scholars agree; the disagreement about its definition actually stems from its extreme clarity, not from obscurity.

الباصرة
انطباع
صور المعقولات
الحد الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example (analogy of vision) is meant to define a particular instance of knowledge, such as knowing that one is half of two.
Correction: The example is meant to illustrate the general nature of knowledge as the impression of intelligible forms in the intellect, not to define a specific piece of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it difficult to define knowledge by a real definition?
A) Because knowledge is obscure and hidden.
B) Because knowledge is too obvious and clear, so nothing is clearer to define it.
C) Because the example used is incorrect.
D) Because Imam al-Razi forbade defining it.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is so self-evident that any attempt at a real definition falls short; the best we can do is provide clarifying examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge of basic truths is so clear that it cannot be defined, why do you still find yourself doubting the foundational beliefs of your faith? Is the problem in your mind or in your heart's willingness to submit?
?
The intellect is described as a mirror. Which of your beliefs might be distorted by your ego or worldly desires, preventing that mirror from reflecting reality as it is? What would it cost you to polish that mirror?
?
Imam al-Razi says disagreement about knowledge stems from its clarity, not obscurity. Think of a specific Islamic teaching you have struggled with. Is it possible that your struggle is not because the truth is hidden, but because it is too clear—and demands a change you are resisting? What change is it asking of you today?

Three Meanings of 'Ilm: An Overview
Sanusi lists the three technical meanings of 'ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct technical usages of the word 'ilm in Islamic scholarship and why it is homonymous.

Core Idea
The word 'ilm has three technical meanings, not one.

Sanusi begins by noting that everyone agrees 'ilm is homonymous. He then lists the three different ways the term is used in technical discourse: as intellectual perception, as a type of assent, and as an umbrella for conception and certain assent. This overview sets the stage for the detailed elucidation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the three numbered items.
The passage begins by stating there is no dispute over homonymy.
2
Identify the three meanings
Find the three distinct definitions: (1) absolute perception of intellect, (2) one division of assent, (3) what encompasses conception and certain assent.
Each is introduced by a number in the text.
3
Understand the rationale
The author briefly explains the first and third meanings with alternative phrasings. Note that the third meaning has two possible definitions.
This lesson is just an overview; detailed commentary follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نزاع في اشتراك لفظه فقد يقال (۱) المطلق إدراك العقل، فيفسر بحصول الصورة في العقل (٢) أو وصول النفس إلى المعنى ولأحد أقسام التصديق فيفسر بالحكم الجازم المطابق الموجب (۳)، ولما يشمل التصور والتصديق اليقيني فيفسر بصفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت به إذ لا تجلي (٤) في غير اليقيني، أو بصفة توجب تمييزاً بين المعاني لا يحتمل النقيض، والعاديات إنما تحتمل النقيض بمعنى أنه لو فرض وقوعه (٥) لم يلزم منه محال لذاته (٦) لا بمعنى تجويز العالم إياه حقيقة كما في الظن أو حكماً كما في اعتقاد المقلد). لفظ العلم يقال في الاصطلاح على معان منها إدراك العقل، فيفسر بحصول صورة الشيء في العقل، وسيجيء في بحث الكيفيات تحقيقه ودفع ما أورد عليه ، وبعضهم نظر إلى أن العلم صفة العالم، والحصول صفة الصورة، فعدل إلى وصول النفس إلى المعنى أخذاً بما ذكره الرازي وغيره، أن أول مراتب وصول النفس إلى المعنى شعور، فإذا حصل وقف النفس على تمام ذلك المعنى فتصور فإذا بقي بحيث لو أراد استرجاعه بعد ذهابه أمكنه يقال له : حفظ، ولذلك الطلب تذكر، ولذلك الوجدان ذكر وأنت خبير بأن حصول الصورة في الفعل أيضاً صفة العالم (۲) ومنها أحد أقسام التصديق وهو ما يقارن الجزم والمطابقة والثبات فيخرج الطن والجهل المركب والتقليد، وسيجيء بيان ذلك . ومنها ما يشمل تصور المُطابق والتصديق اليقيني على ما هو الموافق للعرف واللغة، ولهم فيه عباراتان الأولى : صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت أي صفة ينكشف بها ما يذكر . ويلتفت إليه انكشافاً تاماً لمن قامت به تلك الصفة إنساناً كان أو غيره، وعدل عن الشيء إلى المذكور ليعم الموجود المعدوم، وقد يتوهم أن المراد به العلوم لأن في ذكر العلم ذكر المعلوم وعدل إليه تفادياً عن الدور، وبالجملة فقد خرج الظن والجهل إذ تجلى فيهما، وكذا اعتقاداً المقلد لأنه عقدة على القلب والتجلي وانشراح وانحلال للعقدة الثانية صفة توجب تمييزاً للمعاني لا يحتمل النقيض أي صفة تستعقب لخلق الله تعالى لمن قامت به، تمييزاً في الأمور العقلية كلية كانت أو جزئية، فيخرج مثل القدرة ،والإرادة، وهو ظاهر إدراك الحواس لأن تمييز في الأعيان ومن
And there is no dispute over its homonymy. For it is said (1) absolutely [meaning] the perception of the intellect, and is explained as the obtaining of the form in the intellect, (2) or the soul's arrival at the meaning. And for one of the divisions of assent, it is explained as the decisive, conforming, affirmative judgment. (3) And for what encompasses conception and certain assent, it is explained as an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed to the one in whom it subsists—since there is no disclosure in non-certain things—or as an attribute that necessitates a distinction between meanings that does not admit of contradiction. (As for contingent events, they admit of contradiction in the sense that if their occurrence were supposed, no impossibility would follow from it in itself, not in the sense of the knower's actually deeming it possible as in opinion, or judgmentally as in the belief of the blind follower.) The word 'ilm is used in terminology for meanings including: perception of the intellect, explained as the obtaining of the form of the thing in the intellect, and its verification and refutation of objections will come in the discussion of qualities. And some considered that knowledge is an attribute of the knower, while obtaining is an attribute of the form, so they turned to the soul's arrival at the meaning, taking from what al-Razi and others mentioned: that the first stage of the soul's arrival at the meaning is perception; when it obtains and the soul stops at the entirety of that meaning, it is conception; then if it remains such that if one wanted to retrieve it after its departure, he could, it is called memory; hence its seeking is recollection, and its finding is remembrance. And you are aware that the obtaining of the form in the act is also an attribute of the knower. (2) And among them is one of the divisions of assent, which is what is accompanied by decisiveness, conformity, and stability, so opinion, compound ignorance, and blind following are excluded, and its explanation will come. (3) And among them is what encompasses conforming conception and certain assent, according to what is in accordance with custom and language. And for it there are two expressions: First: an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed to the one in whom it subsists, i.e., an attribute by which what is mentioned is uncovered and turned to, a complete disclosure for the one in whom that attribute subsists, whether human or other. And he turned from 'the thing' to 'the mentioned' to include the existent and non-existent. And it might be thought that what is intended by it is the sciences, because in mentioning knowledge, the known is mentioned, and he turned to it to avoid circularity. In short, opinion and ignorance have exited, since there is disclosure in both, and likewise the belief of the blind follower because it is a knot on the heart, and disclosure is opening and unraveling of the knot. Second: an attribute that necessitates a distinction for meanings that does not admit of contradiction, i.e., an attribute that entails, by the creation of God Most High, for the one in whom it subsists, a distinction in intellectual matters, whether universal or particular, so power and will are excluded, and the perception of the senses is apparent because it is a distinction in concrete objects, and from
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three main terminological usages of the word 'ilm (knowledge). The author first states that everyone agrees the term is homonymous. Then he lists: (1) absolute perception of the intellect, interpreted as the obtaining of the form in the intellect, or alternatively as the soul's arrival at the meaning. A digression follows explaining the stages of the soul's arrival: perception, then conception, then memory. (2) One division of assent (tasdiq): the decisive, conforming, affirmative judgment that excludes opinion, compound ignorance, and blind imitation. (3) What covers both conception (tasawwur) and certain assent (tasdiq yaqini), consistent with common and linguistic usage. For this third meaning, two definitions are given: (a) an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed completely (this excludes opinion and imitation), and (b) an attribute that necessitates a distinction not admitting contradiction (this excludes power, will, and sensory perception). The passage explains the rationales behind these definitions, such as avoiding circularity and including non-existent objects. The text cuts off mid-sentence at the end of the page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'ilm has only one meaning in technical discourse.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that 'ilm is homonymous and used for three distinct technical meanings.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ilm has three distinct technical meanings in Islamic scholarship, and recognizing this is essential for understanding subsequent discussions.

First Meaning: Intellectual Perception
Sanusi details the first meaning of 'ilm with its two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first terminological meaning of 'ilm as intellectual perception, including its interpretation as obtaining a form in the intellect or the soul's arrival at the meaning, and the stages of the soul's arrival.

Core Idea
The first meaning of 'ilm is the intellect's perception of the form or the soul's arrival at the meaning.

After the overview, Sanusi elaborates on the first meaning. He discusses the two interpretations: obtaining the form in the intellect (preferred by some) and the soul's arrival at the meaning (preferred by others to avoid attributing obtaining to the form rather than the knower). He also explains the stages of the soul's arrival, from perception to memory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first meaning
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the two interpretations.
The passage includes a digression on the stages of the soul's arrival.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The first interpretation is 'obtaining the form in the intellect'. The second is 'the soul's arrival at the meaning'. Why might some prefer the second?
The commentary explains the rationale.
3
Follow the stages of the soul's arrival
The author lists: perception, conception, memory, recollection, remembrance. See how these relate to 'ilm.
This is based on al-Razi's analysis.

Second Meaning: Assent (Tasdiq)
Sanusi defines the second meaning of 'ilm as a type of assent with specific conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second terminological meaning of 'ilm as a specific division of assent, characterized by decisiveness, conformity, and stability, and excluding opinion, compound ignorance, and blind imitation.

Core Idea
In its second meaning, 'ilm is decisive, conforming, stable assent—not mere opinion or imitation.

Sanusi now turns to the second meaning, which is a particular kind of assent (tasdiq). This is a technical term in logic and theology. He defines it clearly: it must be accompanied by decisiveness (jasm), conformity (mutabaqa) to reality, and stability (thabat). This excludes three things: opinion (zann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and blind imitation (taqlid).

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage on the second meaning
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the three conditions and the three exclusions.
This is a concise definition.
2
Understand the conditions
The three conditions: decisiveness (jasm), conformity (mutabaqa), stability (thabat). Why is each important?
These ensure the knowledge is certain and true.
3
Identify the exclusions
Opinion, compound ignorance, and blind imitation are excluded because they lack one or more of the conditions.
Details on these exclusions will come later.

And there is no dispute over its homonymy. For it is said (1) absolutely [meaning] the perception of the intellect, and is explained as the obtaining of the form in the intellect, (2) or the soul's arrival at the meaning. And for one of the divisions of assent, it is explained as the decisive, conforming, affirmative judgment. (3) And for what encompasses conception and certain assent, it is explained as an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed to the one in whom it subsists—since there is no disclosure in non-certain things—or as an attribute that necessitates a distinction between meanings that does not admit of contradiction. (As for contingent events, they admit of contradiction in the sense that if their occurrence were supposed, no impossibility would follow from it in itself, not in the sense of the knower's actually deeming it possible as in opinion, or judgmentally as in the belief of the blind follower.) The word 'ilm is used in terminology for meanings including: perception of the intellect, explained as the obtaining of the form of the thing in the intellect, and its verification and refutation of objections will come in the discussion of qualities. And some considered that knowledge is an attribute of the knower, while obtaining is an attribute of the form, so they turned to the soul's arrival at the meaning, taking from what al-Razi and others mentioned: that the first stage of the soul's arrival at the meaning is perception; when it obtains and the soul stops at the entirety of that meaning, it is conception; then if it remains such that if one wanted to retrieve it after its departure, he could, it is called memory; hence its seeking is recollection, and its finding is remembrance. And you are aware that the obtaining of the form in the act is also an attribute of the knower. (2) And among them is one of the divisions of assent, which is what is accompanied by decisiveness, conformity, and stability, so opinion, compound ignorance, and blind following are excluded, and its explanation will come. (3) And among them is what encompasses conforming conception and certain assent, according to what is in accordance with custom and language. And for it there are two expressions: First: an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed to the one in whom it subsists, i.e., an attribute by which what is mentioned is uncovered and turned to, a complete disclosure for the one in whom that attribute subsists, whether human or other. And he turned from 'the thing' to 'the mentioned' to include the existent and non-existent. And it might be thought that what is intended by it is the sciences, because in mentioning knowledge, the known is mentioned, and he turned to it to avoid circularity. In short, opinion and ignorance have exited, since there is disclosure in both, and likewise the belief of the blind follower because it is a knot on the heart, and disclosure is opening and unraveling of the knot. Second: an attribute that necessitates a distinction for meanings that does not admit of contradiction, i.e., an attribute that entails, by the creation of God Most High, for the one in whom it subsists, a distinction in intellectual matters, whether universal or particular, so power and will are excluded, and the perception of the senses is apparent because it is a distinction in concrete objects, and from
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three main terminological usages of the word 'ilm (knowledge). The author first states that everyone agrees the term is homonymous. Then he lists: (1) absolute perception of the intellect, interpreted as the obtaining of the form in the intellect, or alternatively as the soul's arrival at the meaning. A digression follows explaining the stages of the soul's arrival: perception, then conception, then memory. (2) One division of assent (tasdiq): the decisive, conforming, affirmative judgment that excludes opinion, compound ignorance, and blind imitation. (3) What covers both conception (tasawwur) and certain assent (tasdiq yaqini), consistent with common and linguistic usage. For this third meaning, two definitions are given: (a) an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed completely (this excludes opinion and imitation), and (b) an attribute that necessitates a distinction not admitting contradiction (this excludes power, will, and sensory perception). The passage explains the rationales behind these definitions, such as avoiding circularity and including non-existent objects. The text cuts off mid-sentence at the end of the page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think any kind of certainty counts as 'ilm in this sense.
Correction: Only certainty that is decisive, conforming to reality, and stable qualifies.

In its second meaning, 'ilm is a specific division of assent that is decisive, conforming to reality, and stable, excluding opinion, compound ignorance, and blind imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Third Meaning: Conception and Certain Assent
Sanusi defines the third meaning of 'ilm and provides two detailed definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third terminological meaning of 'ilm, which encompasses conception and certain assent, including its two definitions focusing on disclosure and non-contradictory distinction.

Core Idea
The third meaning of 'ilm covers both conception and certain assent, defined as either disclosure or non-contradictory distinction.

Sanusi concludes with the third meaning, which is the broadest, encompassing both conception (tasawwur) of the conforming and certain assent (tasdiq yaqini). This aligns with common and linguistic usage. He provides two definitions: one based on disclosure (tajalli) and one based on distinction (tamyiz). He also explains why opinion and imitation are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third meaning
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the two definitions and the exclusions.
This is the longest part of the lesson.
2
Understand the first definition: disclosure
Ilm is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed completely. This excludes opinion and imitation.
The author explains why the term 'madhkur' is used instead of 'shay'.
3
Understand the second definition: distinction
Ilm is an attribute that necessitates a distinction in intellectual matters that does not admit of contradiction.
This excludes power, will, and sensory perception.
