Stages of the Soul's Arrival at Meaning
Al-Taftazani enumerates stages of the soul's arrival at meaning and refutes a criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five stages of the soul's arrival at meaning as a definition of knowledge, and how al-Taftazani responds to an objection regarding the quality of the knower.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a journey of the soul through sensation, conceptualization, retention, recollection, and remembrance.

Al-Taftazani addresses a criticism of a previous definition of knowledge. In doing so, he presents an alternative model where knowledge is described as the soul's arrival at meaning, broken down into distinct stages. This passage clarifies both the process and the philosophical underpinnings of the concept of knowledge in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the objection
The passage begins by noting an objection to a prior definition. Some scholars argued that knowledge is a quality of the knower, not the image. Read the opening lines carefully to see how al-Taftazani responds.
The term 'al-ṣūra' here refers to the mental form, not a physical image.
2
Identify the five stages of the soul's arrival
The passage lists stages in order: sensation (shu‛ūr), conceptualization (taṣawwur), retention (ḥifẓ), recollection (tadhakkur), and remembrance (dhikr). Note the progression and the conditions for each stage.
The stages are defined by the soul's relationship to the meaning – from initial awareness to permanent recall.
3
Reflect on the final note about the form in action
Al-Taftazani concludes by asserting that even when the form occurs in action, it remains a quality of the knower. This reaffirms the original point despite the objection.
This statement ties back to the objection mentioned at the beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أورد عليه ، وبعضهم نظر إلى أن العلم صفة العالم، والحصول صفة الصورة، فعدل إلى وصول النفس إلى المعنى أخذاً بما ذكره الرازي وغيره، أن أول مراتب وصول النفس إلى المعنى شعور، فإذا حصل وقف النفس على تمام ذلك المعنى فتصور فإذا بقي بحيث لو أراد استرجاعه بعد ذهابه أمكنه يقال له : حفظ، ولذلك الطلب تذكر، ولذلك الوجدان ذكر وأنت خبير بأن حصول الصورة في الفعل أيضاً صفة العالم
It was objected to him, and some considered that knowledge is a quality of the knower, and the form (al-ṣūra) is a quality of the image (al-ṣūra), so he turned to the soul's arrival at the meaning, taking from what al-Rāzī and others mentioned: that the first stages of the soul's arrival at the meaning is sensation (shu‛ūr). Then when it occurs, the soul pauses at the entirety of that meaning, so it is conceptualized (taṣawwur). Then if it remains such that if one wanted to retrieve it after its departure it would be possible, it is called retention (ḥifẓ). For that reason, seeking is recollection (tadhakkur), and finding is remembrance (dhikr). And you are aware that the occurrence of the image in the action is also a quality of the knower.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage addresses a criticism of a previous definition of knowledge. It presents an alternative approach describing knowledge as the soul's arrival at meaning, with specific stages: sensation (shu‛ūr), conceptualization (taṣawwur), retention (ḥifẓ), recollection (tadhakkur), and remembrance (dhikr). The author notes that even when the form (image) is in the action, it remains a quality of the knower. The passage follows the order of the stages and ends by reaffirming the original point.

حفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term al-ṣūra refers to a physical image or external form.
Correction: In this context, al-ṣūra means the mental form or conceptual image that exists in the mind, not a physical object.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first stage of the soul's arrival at the meaning?
A) تصور (taṣawwur)
B) شعور (shu‛ūr)
C) حفظ (ḥifẓ)
D) تذكر (tadhakkur)

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is defined as the soul's arrival at meaning through a progression from sensation to remembrance, and even when the form is in action, it remains a quality of the knower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own learning: do you stop at mere sensation (hearing or reading words) without truly conceptualizing? What would it cost you to remain at that stage and never let the meaning settle in your soul?
?

?
The passage ends by asserting that even the form in action belongs to the knower. Is your knowledge merely stored information, or does it manifest in your actions and character, proving that it has truly arrived in your soul?

Knowledge as Assent with Certainty, Correspondence, and Stability
Al-Taftazani defines knowledge as a type of assent with three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of knowledge in Islamic theology as a specific type of assent that must be certain, correspond to reality, and be stable.

Core Idea
Knowledge is certain, truthful, and unchanging assent — beyond guesswork and blind following.

Al-Taftazani begins by narrowing down what counts as knowledge. Not every belief is knowledge; it must be a firm assent that matches reality and cannot be overturned. This lesson unpacks the three essential conditions and the three common pitfalls that fail to meet them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist of the definition. Notice the key terms: تصديق, جزم, مطابقة, ثبات.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three conditions
Identify jazzm (certainty), muṭābaqa (correspondence), and thabāt (stability). Understand what each adds to the concept of knowledge.
ثبات does not mean mere persistence; it means the judgment is not subject to revision.
3
Notice the exclusions
See why ẓann (conjecture), jahl murakkab (compound ignorance), and taqlīd (blind following) fail the conditions of knowledge.
Each exclusion violates at least one of the three conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أحد أقسام التصديق وهو ما يقارن الجزم والمطابقة والثبات فيخرج الظن والجهل المركب والتقليد، وسيجيء بيان ذلك
And among them is one of the divisions of assent (taṣdīq), which is what is accompanied by certainty (jazzm), correspondence (muṭābaqa), and stability (thabāt). So conjecture (ẓann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and blind following (taqlīd) are excluded. And its explanation will come later.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge as a specific type of assent (taṣdīq) that must be accompanied by three conditions: certainty (jazzm) that excludes doubt, correspondence (muṭābaqa) to reality, and stability (thabāt) such that it is not subject to change. It then lists what is excluded: conjecture (ẓann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and blind following (taqlīd). The author notes that a detailed explanation will follow.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'ثبات' (thabāt) might be confused with mere persistence or continuity; some might think it simply means the belief lasts over time.
Correction: In this definition, thabāt means the judgment is not subject to revision or change — it is stable in the sense of being conclusive and not open to doubt, not merely that it persists.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT excluded from the definition of knowledge according to this passage?
A) الظن (conjecture)
B) الجهل المركب (compound ignorance)
C) التقليد (blind following)
D) اليقين (certainty)

Key Takeaway
Knowledge in Islamic theology is defined as certain, correspondent, and stable assent, excluding conjecture, compound ignorance, and blind following.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one deeply held belief you are certain of. When was the last time you honestly examined whether it perfectly matches reality — not just feels right? If you never have, can you truly call it knowledge?
?
Is there a belief you hold simply because a teacher or parent instilled it in you, and you've never questioned it? The passage says blind following (taqlīd) is excluded from knowledge. Will you let that unsettle you today and dare to seek evidence for yourself?
?
Consider a belief you once held with absolute certainty but later abandoned. What did it lack — correspondence, stability, or both? If true knowledge cannot change, what does that demand of the way you hold your current convictions?

The First Definition: Knowledge as Manifestation
First definition of knowledge with clarifications and exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first definition of knowledge in kalam: its wording, rationale, and how it excludes conjecture, ignorance, and blind following.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the quality that fully discloses the mentioned, whether existent or nonexistent.

Al-Taftazani begins his discussion of knowledge by presenting the first of two definitions. This definition captures the essential function of knowledge: it makes the known object manifest to the knower. The passage carefully explains the choice of words, addresses a potential circularity, and clarifies what kinds of cognition are not knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud if possible, noting the key terms 'تجلي' and 'انكشاف'.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the core definition
The definition states knowledge is a quality by which 'the mentioned' is fully disclosed to the one possessing it. Notice that it says 'the mentioned' (المذكور) rather than 'the thing' (الشيء).
This choice is intentional to include non-existents.
3
Analyze the rationale
The author explains why he uses 'المذكور' and refutes the misunderstanding that it refers to the sciences (to avoid circularity).
Circularity would arise if knowledge were defined by 'the known'.
4
Identify the exclusions
The passage explicitly excludes conjecture (ظن), ignorance (جهل), and the belief of the blind follower (تقليد). Understand why each is excluded.
These lack complete manifestation or involve a knot on the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم فيه عبارتان الأولى : صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت أي صفة ينكشف بها ما يذكر . ويلتفت إليه انكشافاً تاماً لمن قامت به تلك الصفة إنساناً كان أو غيره، وعدل عن الشيء إلى المذكور ليعم الموجود المعدوم، وقد يتوهم أن المراد به العلوم لأن في ذكر العلم ذكر المعلوم وعدل إليه تفادياً عن الدور، وبالجملة فقد خرج الظن والجهل إذ تجلى فيهما، وكذا اعتقاد المقلد لأنه عقدة على القلب والتجلي وانشراح وانحلال للعقدة
And they have two expressions regarding it. The first: a quality by which the mentioned (al-madhkūr) is manifested to the one in whom it exists—i.e., a quality by which what is mentioned is disclosed and attended to with complete disclosure for the one who possesses that quality, whether human or other. And he turned from 'the thing' to 'the mentioned' to include both existent and nonexistent. It might be thought that what is meant is the sciences because mentioning 'knowledge' includes the known, but he turned to it to avoid circularity. In short, conjecture and ignorance are excluded because they do not manifest, and likewise the belief of the blind follower because it is a knot upon the heart, while manifestation is an expansion and untying of the knot.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage gives the first of two definitions of knowledge: it is a quality by which the mentioned thing (al-madhkūr) is fully manifested to the one who has that quality. It explains that the phrase 'the mentioned' is used instead of 'the thing' to include both existent and nonexistent. It also addresses a potential misunderstanding that it refers to the sciences, but clarifies that it avoids circularity. The definition excludes conjecture and ignorance because they do not involve manifestation, and excludes blind following because it is a knot on the heart, whereas manifestation is an expansion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'المذكور' only refers to existent things or to the sciences themselves.
Correction: The text explicitly states that 'المذكور' is used to include both existent and nonexistent, and that it is not intended to mean sciences (which would cause circularity).

Checkpoint

B) To include both existent and nonexistent

Key Takeaway
The first definition of knowledge emphasizes complete disclosure of the object, whether existent or not, excluding incomplete cognition and blind acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Truth is disclosed completely to the knower. When you say you 'know' something about Allah or His attributes, can you honestly say it is from complete disclosure, or is it merely inherited belief? What step will you take to move from habit to genuine knowledge?
?
The definition includes knowledge of non-existents. How often do you reflect on what is absent—like the non-existence of partners with Allah, or the absence of doubt in your heart? Does your certainty expand or contract when you consider the unseen?

Knowledge as a Distinction Not Admitting Opposition
Al-Taftazani explains the second definition of knowledge and its exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of knowledge as a distinction that does not admit its opposite, and how it excludes various cognitive states such as conjecture, doubt, and compound ignorance.

Core Idea
True knowledge is a clear distinction that leaves no room for its opposite.

We now turn to the second definition of knowledge, which centers on the concept of ‘distinction’ (tamyīz) that excludes the possibility of its opposite. This definition helps separate genuine knowledge from mere opinion, doubt, and other incomplete states. Let's unpack it step by step, following al-Taftazani's precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its explanation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the definition is: 'a quality that necessitates a distinction of meanings that does not admit the opposite.' Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يحتمل النقيض' (does not admit the opposite).
The definition focuses on the quality itself, not the subject.
2
Identify what the definition excludes
The author explicitly excludes power, will, and sense perception (if taken as knowledge of sensibles). Note why each is excluded: they do not provide a distinction of meanings in intellectual matters.
Sense perception is excluded because it distinguishes external objects, not meanings.
3
Understand why other cognitive states are excluded
Conjecture, doubt, illusion, compound ignorance, and blind following are all excluded because they admit the opposite. Read the reasons given for each.
Compound ignorance is said to be 'not a distinction' and also admits opposition.
4
Consider the objections and clarifications
The passage addresses potential objections: compound ignorance is not a distinction; non-corresponding conceptualization is also not. But corresponding conceptualization is included because it has no opposite.
The author notes that taking the opposite involves judgment and composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية صفة توجب تمييزاً للمعاني لا يحتمل النقيض أي صفة تستعقب لخلق الله تعالى لمن قامت به، تمييزاً في الأمور العقلية كلية كانت أو جزئية، فيخرج مثل القدرة والإرادة، وهو ظاهر إدراك الحواس لأن تمييز في الأعيان ومن جعله علماً بالمحسوسات لم يذكر هذا القيد وخرج سائر الإدراكات، لأن احتمال النقيض في الظن والشك والوهم ظاهر وفي الجهل المركب أظهر ، وكذا اعتقاد المقلد لأنه يزول بتشكيك المشكك بل ربما يتعلق بالنقيض جزماً، وقد يقال إن الجهل المركب ليس بتمييز، وكذا التصور الغير المطابق كما إذا ارتسم في النفس من الفرس صورة حيوان ناطق، وأما المطابق فداخل لأنه لا نقيض له بناء على أن في أخذ النقيض شائبة في الحكم والتركيب، ولا يخفى ما فيه
The second: a quality that necessitates a distinction (tamyīz) of meanings that does not admit the opposite (naqīḍ)—i.e., a quality that entails, by God's creation, for the one who possesses it, a distinction in intellectual matters, whether universal or particular. Thus power and will are excluded, and likewise sense perception because it is a distinction of external objects, and if one makes it knowledge of sensibles, he did not mention this restriction. And other perceptions are excluded because the probability of opposition in conjecture, doubt, and illusion is apparent, and in compound ignorance even more so. Likewise the belief of the blind follower because it is removed by the skeptic's doubt; rather, it might even attach to the opposite with certainty. It may be said that compound ignorance is not a distinction, likewise non-corresponding conceptualization—as when a form of a speaking animal is imprinted in the soul from a horse. As for the corresponding, it is included because it has no opposite, on the basis that taking the opposite has a trace of judgment and composition. And what is in it is not hidden.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the second definition of knowledge: a quality that necessitates a distinction (tamyīz) of meanings that does not admit the opposite (naqīḍ). It explains the conditions, that it applies to intellectual matters whether universal or particular, and then excludes other entities: power, will, sense perception, conjecture, doubt, illusion, compound ignorance, blind following, and non-corresponding conceptualizations. It also addresses potential objections, such as whether compound ignorance is a distinction, and clarifies that corresponding conceptualizations are included because they have no opposite.

Key Terms
تمييز
معاني
احتمال النقيض
ظن
شك
وهم
جهل مركب
تقليد
تصور غير مطابق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'لا يحتمل النقيض' is thought to refer to the quality itself rather than the distinction it necessitates.
Correction: The text specifies that the distinction (tamyīz) is what does not admit the opposite, not the quality itself. The quality is the cause of that distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the second definition, why is compound ignorance excluded?
A) It is a distinction but admits opposition
B) It is not a distinction and admits opposition
C) It involves certainty
D) It corresponds to reality

Key Takeaway
The second definition of knowledge requires a distinction that cannot admit its opposite, thereby excluding all states that fall short of certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a skeptic challenges a religious belief you hold, does it unsettle you? If so, what does that reveal about whether your belief is knowledge according to this lesson? Will you seek to transform it into a distinction that cannot be shaken?

?
Have you ever been certain about something in your deen, only to realize later that it was based on flawed assumptions? How does the concept of 'compound ignorance' call you to humility and deeper investigation into your own convictions?

Refinement of the Second Definition and Comprehensiveness
Al-Taftazani refines the second definition and discusses the principle of comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second definition of kalam is refined through the restriction to universals and the principle of comprehensiveness (ṭard al-ta‛rīf), and how the phrase 'does not admit the opposite' is correctly interpreted.

Core Idea
The definition's comprehensiveness ensures it covers all instances, while the opposite-probability is excluded by certainty.

Al-Taftazani continues to refine the second definition of kalam. Some scholars restrict it to universal meanings to distinguish knowledge from recognition. He then discusses the grammatical principle of comprehensiveness (ṭard al-ta‛rīf) and clarifies that the phrase 'does not admit the opposite' pertains to the object of distinction, not the quality itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the refinement to universals
Read how some scholars restrict the definition to universals to differentiate knowledge from recognition of particulars. Notice why this avoids an objection from al-Mawāqif.
The objection is that the addition of 'universals' is redundant.
2
Grasp the principle of comprehensiveness
Learn what 'ṭard al-ta‛rīf' means: the definition must apply to all individuals of the defined. Ibn al-Ḥājib's grammatical illustration using active participle and present tense verb helps explain this.
This is a key concept in definition theory borrowed from grammar.
3
Interpret 'لا يحتمل النقيض' correctly
Al-Taftazani rejects the interpretation that the quality itself does not admit the opposite, and instead affirms that it concerns the object of distinction: belief with certainty is knowledge, while weak probability is conjecture.
The passage ends abruptly, but the intended meaning is clear from context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قيد المعاني بالكلية ميلا إلى تخصيص العلم بالكليات والمعرفة بالجزئيات فلا يرد ما ذكر في المواقف أن هذه الزيادة مع الغنى عنها محل نظر بطرد التعريف، أي جريانه في جميع أفراد المعرف على ما ذكر ابن الحاجب أن اسم الفاعل يورد على طرد تعريف الاسم، والفعل المضارع على عكسه قالوا : هذا مصطلح النحاة. ثم الظاهر من قولنا تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض أن يراد نقيض التمييز ولما لم يكن له كثير معنى ذهب بعضهم إلى أن المراد أنه صفة توجب التمييز إيجاباً لا يتحمل النقيض، وليس بشيء والحق اعتبار ذلك في متعلق التمييز على ما قالوا إن اعتقاد الشيء مع أنه لا يكون إلا كذا، علم، واحتمال ألا يكون كذا احتمالاً مرجوحا ظن فالمعنى أنه صفة توجب للنفس
And among them are those who restricted meanings to universals (kulliyāt), inclining toward specifying knowledge for universals and recognition for particulars, so that what was mentioned in al-Mawāqif—that this addition along with self-sufficiency is subject to consideration—does not apply. [This is] by the comprehensiveness (ṭard) of the definition, i.e., its running over all individuals of the defined, as Ibn al-Ḥājib mentioned: that the active participle is used for the comprehensiveness of the definition of the noun, and the present tense verb for its opposite; they said: this is the terminology of the grammarians. Then it is apparent from our saying 'a distinction that does not admit the opposite' that what is intended is the opposite of the distinction. And since it does not have much meaning, some went to that what is meant is that it is a quality that necessitates the distinction positively, not bearing the opposite. But this is nothing. The truth is to consider that in the object of the distinction, as they said: the belief in a thing while it is not except such is knowledge, and the probability that it is not such, a weak probability, is conjecture. So the meaning is that it is a quality that necessitates for the soul...
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage discusses a refinement of the second definition: some restrict the meanings to universals (kulliyāt) to differentiate knowledge (of universals) from recognition (of particulars). It then addresses the principle of 'ṭard al-ta‛rīf' (comprehensiveness) as mentioned by Ibn al-Ḥājib, which is a grammatical principle ensuring the definition covers all instances. It then clarifies the phrase 'does not admit the opposite', rejecting the interpretation that it refers to the quality itself, and instead affirming that it pertains to the object of distinction: belief with certainty is knowledge, while weak probability is conjecture.

Key Terms
كليات
جزئيات
طرد التعريف
ابن الحاجب
احتمال النقيض
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the phrase 'لا يحتمل النقيض' means the quality itself positively necessitates distinction and does not admit the opposite.
Correction: The correct interpretation is that it pertains to the object of distinction: belief with certainty is knowledge, while weak probability is conjecture.

Checkpoint

A) The definition's restriction to universals
B) The definition's comprehensiveness covering all individuals of the defined
C) The exclusion of particulars
D) The grammatical analysis of verbs

Key Takeaway
The definition of kalam must be comprehensive and exclude opposite probabilities; the phrase 'does not admit the opposite' applies to the object of distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Knowledge and Its Scope
Author defines knowledge and lists exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial definition of knowledge and what types of cognition it excludes.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a discernment that leaves no room for its opposite.

The author begins by presenting the core definition of knowledge (al-ʿilm) as a discernment (tamyīz) that does not admit its opposite (naqīḍ). He immediately clarifies that this definition excludes many ordinary perceptions, such as opinion, doubt, and illusion, because they all admit the possibility of the opposite. Even the belief of a blind follower is excluded, as it can be unsettled by doubt. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding what qualifies as true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage that states the definition of knowledge. Then read its translation carefully.
Notice that the definition is compact: 'discernment that does not admit the opposite'.
2
Identify the exclusions
The author lists several types of cognition that are excluded from this definition because they admit the opposite. Identify each one: opinion (ẓann), doubt (shakk), illusion (wahm), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and blind follower (muqallid).
Compound ignorance is not a discernment at all, so it is excluded on two counts.
3
Understand the condition about universals
Some scholars restrict knowledge to universals (kulliyāt) and cognition to particulars. The author notes this but suggests it is unnecessary. Also, note the grammatical point about 'thoroughness' (ṭard) of the definition.
This part shows the ongoing refinement of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعله علماً بالمحسوسات لم يذكر هذا القيد وخرج سائر الإدراكات، لأن احتمال النقيض في الظن والشك والوهم ظاهر وفي الجهل المركب أظهر ، وكذا اعتقاد المقلد لأنه يزول بتشكيك المشكك بل ربما يتعلق بالنقيض جزماً، وقد يقال إن الجهل المركب ليس بتمييز، وكذا التصور الغير المطابق كما إذا ارتسم في النفس من الفرس صورة حيوان ناطق، وأما المطابق فداخل لأنه لا نقيض له بناء على أن في أخذ النقيض شائبة في الحكم والتركيب، ولا يخفى ما فيه ومنهم من قيد المعاني بالكلية ميلا إلى تخصيص العلم بالكليات والمعرفة بالجزئيات فلا يرد ما ذكر في المواقف أن هذه الزيادة مع الغنى عنها محل نظر) بطرد التعريف، أي جريانه في جميع أفراد المعرف على ما ذكر ابن الحاجب أن اسم الفاعل يورد على طرد تعريف الاسم، والفعل المضارع على عكسه قالوا : هذا مصطلح النحاة. ثم الظاهر من قولنا تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض أن يراد نقيض التمييز ولما لم يكن له كثير معنى ذهب بعضهم إلى أن المراد أنه صفة توجب التمييز إيجاباً لا يتحمل النقيض، وليس بشيء والحق اعتبار ذلك في متعلق التمييز على ما قالوا إن اعتقاد الشيء مع أنه لا يكون إلا كذا، علم، واحتمال ألا يكون كذا احتمالاً مرجوحا ظن فالمعنى أنه صفة توجب للنفس تمييز المعنى عندها بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض في متعلقه، ويدل على ذلك تقرير اعتراضهم بالعلوم العادية مثل العلم بكون الجبل حجراً فإنه يحتمل النقيض بأن لا يكون حجراً بل قد ينقلب ذهباً بأن يخلق الله تعالى مكان الحجر الذهب على ما هو رأي المحققين أو بأن يسلب عن أجزاء الحجر الوصف الذي به صارت حجراً، ويخلق فيه الوصف الذي به يصير ذهباً على ما هو رأي بعض المتكلمين من تجانس الجواهر في جميع الأجسام. والجواب : أن المراد بعدم احتمال النقيض في العلم هو عدم تجويز العالم إياه لا حقيقة وحكماً، أما في التصور فلعدم النقيض أو لأنه لا معنى لاحتمال النقيض بدون شائبة الحكم ، وأما في التصديق فلاستناد جزمه بالحكم إلى موجب بحيث لا يحتمل الزوال أصلا والعاديات كذلك لأن الجزم بها مستند إلى موجب هو العادة . وإنما يحتمل النقيض بمعنى أنه لو فرض وقوعه لم يلزم منه محال لذاته نفسه لكونه في من الممكنات التي يجوز وقوعها أو لا وقوعها، وذلك كما يحكم ببياض الجسم الشاهد قطعاً مع أنه في نفسه ممكن أن يكون وأن لا يكون . والحاصل أن معنى احتمال النقيض تجويز الحاكم إياه حقيقة وحالاً كما في الظن، لعدم الجزم بمتعلقه أو حكماً، ومآلاً، كما في اعتقاد المقلد لعدم استناد الجزم به إلى موجب من حس أو عقل أو عادة فيجوز أن يزول بل يحصل اعتقاد النقيض جزماً. وبهذا يظهر الجواب عن نقض تفسير العلم باعتقاد المقلد سيما فإنه لا يحتمل النقيض في الواقع ولا عند الحاكم . وهو ظاهر ولا عبرة بالإمكان العقلي كما في العاديات .
He made it knowledge of sensibles; he did not mention this condition, and it excludes all other perceptions, because the possibility of the opposite in opinion, doubt, and illusion is apparent, and in compound ignorance more apparent, likewise the belief of the blind follower because it is removed by the doubter's casting doubt, rather it may even be opposite with certainty. And it may be said that compound ignorance is not a discernment, likewise the non-conforming conception as when there is depicted in the soul of a horse an image of a speaking animal. As for the conforming, it is included because it has no opposite, based on the fact that taking the opposite involves a taint of judgment and composition. And it is not hidden what is in it. And among them are those who conditioned meanings by universality tending to specify knowledge with universals and cognition with particulars, so that the objection mentioned in al-Mawāqif that this addition, despite being unnecessary, is debatable does not apply, by the thoroughness of the definition, i.e., its running through all individuals of the defined, as Ibn al-Ḥājib mentioned that the active participle is presented for the thoroughness of the definition of the noun, and the present tense verb for its opposite. They said: this is the terminology of grammarians. Then the apparent from our saying 'a discernment that does not admit the opposite' is that the opposite of discernment is intended, and since it has no much meaning, some went to that what is meant is that it is an attribute that necessitates discernment positively and does not bear the opposite, and it is nothing. And the truth is considering that in the object of discernment, as they said: the belief of a thing while it is only so is knowledge, and the possibility that it is not so with weak probability is opinion. So the meaning is that it is an attribute that necessitates for the soul the discernment of the meaning such that it does not admit the opposite in its object. And evidence for that is the presentation of their objection regarding the ordinary sciences like the knowledge that the mountain is stone, for it admits the opposite that it may not be stone but might turn into gold by God creating gold in place of stone according to the view of the investigators, or by stripping from the parts of stone the description by which it became stone and creating in it the description by which it becomes gold according to the view of some theologians of the homogeneity of substances in all bodies. And the answer: that what is meant by the absence of probability of the opposite in knowledge is the non-permissibility of it by the knower in reality and in judgment. As for conception, due to the absence of an opposite or because there is no meaning of probability of the opposite without a taint of judgment. And as for assent, because his certainty of the judgment is based on a cause such that it does not admit removal at all, and the ordinary things are likewise because the certainty of them is based on a cause which is habit. And the opposite is only probable in the sense that if its occurrence were assumed, no impossibility would follow from it in itself, because it is among the possibles whose occurrence or non-occurrence is permissible. That is like judging the whiteness of a present body with certainty although in itself it is possible to be or not to be. The upshot is that the meaning of probability of the opposite is the permission of it by the judge in reality and currently as in opinion, due to lack of certainty of its object, or in judgment and ultimately, as in the belief of the blind follower due to lack of dependence of his certainty on a cause from sense, reason, or habit, so it is possible that it disappears, rather he obtains certainty of the opposite. And by this the answer becomes clear to the refutation of the definition of knowledge by the belief of the blind follower, especially since it does not admit the opposite in reality nor in the judge's view. And it is apparent, and no regard is given to rational possibility as in the ordinary sciences.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage defends the definition of knowledge (al-ʿilm) as 'a discernment (tamyīz) that does not admit its opposite (lā yaḥtamil al-naqīḍ)'. It addresses several subtle points and objections.

The author begins by noting a condition omitted from an earlier definition: the restriction to sensibles. Without that condition, the definition excludes all other perceptions because the possibility of the opposite is obvious in opinion (ẓann), doubt (shakk), illusion (wahm), and especially in compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). The belief of a blind follower (iʿtiqād al-muqallid) is also excluded because it can be unsettled by a skeptic. Compound ignorance is said not to be a discernment because it is mistaken; likewise non-conforming conceptions (like imagining a horse as a speaking animal). Conforming conceptions are included because they have no opposite.

The author then discusses a view that restricts knowledge to universals and cognition to particulars, but this is deemed unnecessary. He quotes Ibn al-Ḥājib's grammatical point that the active participle (ism fāʿil) is used for thoroughness (ṭard) of the noun definition, while the present verb is used for its opposite.

The core clarification: 'not admitting the opposite' refers to the object of discernment (mutaʿallaq al-tamyīz). Belief that a thing is only such is knowledge; weak probability that it is otherwise is opinion. Thus knowledge is an attribute that necessitates discernment of a meaning such that the opposite is not admitted in its object.

The main objection concerns ordinary sciences (al-ʿulūm al-ʿādiyya), e.g., knowing that a mountain is stone. In reality, it could become gold (by God's power). So how does it not admit the opposite? The answer: 'not admitting the opposite' means the knower does not allow it in actual judgment, not that it is impossible in reality. In conception, there is no opposite; in assent, certainty is based on a cause (mūjib) that prevents actual removal. Ordinary knowledge is based on the cause of habit (al-ʿāda). The opposite is only possible rationally (imkān ʿaqlī), but this does not affect the definition.

The author summarizes: probability of the opposite means the judge's actual permission of it, as in opinion (lack of certainty) or in the blind follower (lack of a sound cause). The blind follower's belief does not admit the opposite in reality or in his mind, but it is not based on a proper cause, so it is not knowledge. Rational possibility alone is disregarded, as in ordinary sciences.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm)
تمييز (tamyīz)
نقيض (naqīḍ)
ظن (ẓann)
شك (shakk)
وهم (wahm)
جهل مركب (jahl murakkab)
مقلد (muqallid)
تصور (taṣawwur)
تصديق (taṣdīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all kinds of cognition, including opinion and doubt, count as knowledge because they involve some discernment.
Correction: Knowledge requires that the discernment does not admit the opposite. Opinion, doubt, etc., admit the opposite, so they are not knowledge.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT excluded from the definition of knowledge according to this passage?
A) Opinion (ẓann)
B) Doubt (shakk)
C) Conforming conception (al-taṣawwur al-muṭābiq)
D) Illusion (wahm)

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a special discernment that does not tolerate its opposite, excluding all other forms of cognition that do.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you hold opinions (ẓann) with certainty, even though they admit the possibility of error? How can you distinguish between true knowledge and mere strong opinion?
?
Do you rely on the belief of a blind follower (muqallid) in matters of faith? What would it take for you to move from inherited certainty to personal discernment that excludes doubt?
?
Your heart may be convinced of something that is actually false (compound ignorance). When was the last time you discovered you were absolutely certain about something that turned out wrong? What will you do to actively expose yourself to correction?

Refining the Meaning of 'No Opposite'
Author clarifies 'no opposite' and answers objection from ordinary sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refined interpretation of 'no opposite' in the definition of knowledge and how it handles objections from ordinary sciences.

Core Idea
The knower does not allow the opposite in judgment, even if it is rationally possible.

Having established the definition, the author now refines the key phrase 'does not admit the opposite'. He clarifies that it means the knower does not permit the opposite in his actual judgment, not that the opposite is impossible in external reality. This is crucial to defend against the objection from ordinary sciences (al-ʿulūm al-ʿādiyya), such as knowing that a mountain is stone, which could become gold by God's power. The answer distinguishes between rational possibility and the absence of actual permission in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'ثم الظاهر من قولنا تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض' through the end. Follow the translation.
This part is dense; go slowly.
2
Understand the refined meaning
The phrase 'no opposite' is explained to refer to the object of discernment. Knowledge is an attribute that necessitates discernment such that the opposite is not admitted in its object. This means the knower does not entertain the opposite in his judgment.
The example of belief that a thing is only such is knowledge; weak probability of alternative is opinion.
3
Analyze the objection from ordinary sciences
The objection is that ordinary knowledge (e.g., mountain is stone) admits the opposite because God could change it. The author presents the objection and then the answer.
The answer: 'not admitting the opposite' means the knower does not allow it in actual judgment, not that it is impossible externally.
4
Distinguish rational possibility from actual permission
Rational possibility (imkān ʿaqlī) does not affect knowledge. The opposite is only probable in the sense that if it occurred, no logical contradiction would follow, but the knower does not entertain it.
This distinction resolves the objection.

He made it knowledge of sensibles; he did not mention this condition, and it excludes all other perceptions, because the possibility of the opposite in opinion, doubt, and illusion is apparent, and in compound ignorance more apparent, likewise the belief of the blind follower because it is removed by the doubter's casting doubt, rather it may even be opposite with certainty. According to the passage, why does the knowledge that a mountain is stone not admit the opposite, even though God could change it to gold?
A) Because the opposite is impossible in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own convictions, are there beliefs you hold with certainty that are actually based on blind following (taqlid) rather than on a cause from sense, reason, or habit? How would you know the difference, and what would it cost you to honestly examine them?
?
The author says that in ordinary sciences, certainty is based on habit (ʿādah). Is your daily trust in the reliability of the world—like that the sun will rise—a form of knowledge or just a comfortable habit? If it is habit, what does that say about the foundation of your faith in Allah's promises which also rely on His habit?
?
Consider a belief you hold that seems impossible to change—like the existence of God. The lesson says that knowledge requires that the opposite is not permitted by the knower, even if rational possibility exists. Do you actually *permit* the possibility that God does not exist in your heart, or have you closed that door with certainty? If you have not closed it, is it truly knowledge? If you have, what is your evidence (mujib) for that certainty?

Clarifying 'No Possibility of Contradiction' in the Definition of Knowledge
Author refutes objection against definition of knowledge as not admitting contradiction, using customary knowledge example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'no possibility of contradiction' in the definition of knowledge, distinguishing between the knower's conviction and objective possibility.

Core Idea
Knowledge means the knower does not entertain the opposite, not that the opposite is impossible in reality.

Sanusi responds to an objection against defining knowledge as that which does not admit contradiction. Critics point to customary knowledge like 'a mountain is stone' which could change. This lesson unpacks his answer clarifying what 'no possibility of contradiction' truly means—a matter of the knower's certainty, not logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objection uses customary knowledge to challenge the definition. Understand the hypothetical transformations (stone to gold) as possible by God's power.
This objection assumes 'no possibility' means absolute impossibility.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
Sanusi distinguishes between conception and assent. In conception, there is no judgment to contradict; in assent, certainty is based on a firm cause. See how he applies this to customary knowledge.
The cause for customary knowledge is custom (ada).
3
Contrast knowledge with conjecture and blind following
The lesson concludes by showing that genuine knowledge is based on sense, intellect, or custom, while conjecture and blind imitation lack a firm basis and can admit contradiction in the knower's mind.
This resolves the initial objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمييز المعنى عندها بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض في متعلقه، ويدل على ذلك تقرير اعتراضهم بالعلوم العادية مثل العلم بكون الجبل حجراً فإنه يحتمل النقيض بأن لا يكون حجراً بل قد ينقلب ذهباً بأن يخلق الله تعالى مكان الحجر الذهب على ما هو رأي المحققين أو بأن يسلب عن أجزاء الحجر الوصف الذي به صارت حجراً، ويخلق فيه الوصف الذي به يصير ذهباً على ما هو رأي بعض المتكلمين من تجانس الجواهر في جميع الأجسام. والجواب : أن المراد بعدم احتمال النقيض في العلم هو عدم تجويز العالم إياه لا حقيقة وحكماً، أما في التصور فلعدم النقيض أو لأنه لا معنى لاحتمال النقيض بدون شائبة الحكم ، وأما في التصديق فلاستناد جزمه بالحكم إلى موجب بحيث لا يحتمل الزوال أصلا والعاديات كذلك لأن الجزم بها مستند إلى موجب هو العادة . وإنما يحتمل النقيض بمعنى أنه لو فرض وقوعه لم يلزم منه محال لذاته نفسه لكونه في من الممكنات التي يجوز وقوعها أو لا وقوعها، وذلك كما يحكم ببياض الجسم الشاهد قطعاً مع أنه في نفسه ممكن أن يكون وأن لا يكون . والحاصل أن معنى احتمال النقيض تجويز الحاكم إياه حقيقة وحالاً كما في الظن، لعدم الجزم بمتعلقه أو حكماً، ومآلاً، كما في اعتقاد المقلد لعدم استناد الجزم به إلى موجب من حس أو عقل أو عادة فيجوز أن يزول بل يحصل اعتقاد النقيض جزماً. وبهذا يظهر الجواب عن نقض تفسير العلم باعتقاد المقلد سيما فإنه لا يحتمل النقيض في الواقع ولا عند الحاكم . وهو ظاهر ولا عبرة بالإمكان العقلي كما في العاديات.
The differentiation of the meaning in such a way that it does not admit contradiction in its object. Evidence for this is their objection using customary sciences, such as the knowledge that a mountain is stone, for it admits contradiction in that it may not be stone but may turn into gold by God creating gold in place of stone, according to the opinion of the investigators, or by stripping the parts of stone of the description by which they are stone and creating in them the description by which they become gold, according to the opinion of some theologians that atoms are homogeneous in all bodies. The answer: That what is meant by 'no possibility of contradiction' in knowledge is that the knower does not consider it possible in reality, not in reality and judgment. As for conception, it is because there is no contradiction or because there is no meaning to possibility of contradiction without an admixture of judgment. As for assent, it is because his certainty in the judgment is based on a cause such that it does not admit of disappearance at all. Customary matters are likewise, because certainty in them is based on a cause which is custom. Rather, possibility of contradiction is in the sense that if its occurrence were assumed, no impossibility would follow from it in itself, since it is among possibles that may or may not occur. This is like judging with certainty the whiteness of an observed body, while it is in itself possible that it be or not be. The conclusion is that the meaning of possibility of contradiction is the judge's considering it possible in reality and presently, as in conjecture, for lack of certainty in its object; or in judgment and final outcome, as in the belief of a blind follower, because his certainty is not based on a cause from sense, intellect, or custom, so it may disappear, and indeed he may acquire certain belief in the opposite. By this the answer to the refutation of defining knowledge as the belief of a blind follower becomes clear, especially since it does not admit contradiction in reality nor according to the judge. This is obvious, and no attention is paid to rational possibility, as in customary matters.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage regarding the definition of knowledge as that which does not admit contradiction. The author presents an objection: customary knowledge, like knowing a mountain is stone, seems to admit contradiction because God could change it into gold. The answer clarifies that 'no possibility of contradiction' means the knower does not entertain the opposite as possible, not that the opposite is impossible in reality. The author distinguishes between conception and assent. In conception, there is no judgment so no contradiction; in assent, certainty is based on a firm cause. Customary knowledge is certain based on custom, but it is still possible in itself. The conclusion distinguishes between genuine knowledge (based on sense, intellect, or custom) and mere belief of a blind follower, which lacks a proper basis and can change. Rational possibility does not affect the definition.

Key Terms
علم
نقيض
ظن
اعتقاد المقلد
عادة
تجويز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'no possibility of contradiction' means the opposite is logically impossible or cannot happen in reality.
Correction: The author clarifies it means the knower does not consider the opposite possible, not that it is impossible in reality. Customary knowledge, while certain to the knower, is still possible in itself to change by God's power.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'no possibility of contradiction' (la yahtamil al-naqid) in the definition of knowledge according to the author?
A) The object cannot change in reality.
B) The knower does not consider it possible that the opposite occurs.
C) It is logically impossible.
D) It is only possible in customary matters.

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge as not admitting contradiction refers to the knower's conviction, not objective impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a belief you hold with unwavering certainty. What is its basis—sense, intellect, or custom? If it is custom, does the awareness that God could alter that custom unsettle your certainty? How do you reconcile that possibility with the definition of knowledge as 'no possibility of contradiction'? Are you actually a knower or just a blind follower?
?
Think of a time you lost a belief you were certain of. Did that experience reveal that your belief was not genuine knowledge according to this lesson? What would it take for your current deepest convictions to vanish? Are you building them on a firm cause, or merely repeating inherited opinions?
?
You may claim to know that God exists, but is your certainty based on a true cause (intellect, revelation) or on habit? If it were challenged by a powerful doubt, would your conviction withstand it? This lesson challenges you to examine the foundation of your faith: is it mere blind following or genuine knowledge that does not admit the possibility of the opposite?

Division of Knowledge into Conception and Assent
Author presents division of knowledge and modification to bare conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of knowledge into conception and assent, and the modification to bare conception to resolve the interdependence issue.

Core Idea
Conception and assent are interdependent, so scholars modify conception to bare conception.

The famous division of knowledge into conception and assent seems straightforward, but it faces a logical challenge: you cannot have assent without conception, and real conception involves some judgment. In this passage, the author presents this division and explains how scholars resolve the problem by introducing the concept of 'bare conception' — conception without any judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without analyzing. Let the author's own presentation come through.
Notice the compact style and the use of key terms.
2
Understand the objection
Reflect on why some rejected the division: the interdependence between conception and assent. Why would the division be problematic?
This is a classic logical issue in Islamic epistemology.
3
Learn the modification
See how scholars changed the definition of conception to 'bare conception' (tasawwur sādhhij) to make the division valid.
Bare conception is conditioned by absence of any judgment.
4
Grasp the key terms
Identify and understand the Arabic terms: تصور, تصديق, تصور ساذج, حكم, إذعان.
These terms are foundational for further study in logic and kalam.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm that you understood the modification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : العلم إن كان حكماً أي إذعاناً وقبولاً للنسبة فتصديق وإلا فتصور واختلافهما بالحقيقة لا بمجرد الإضافة). أقول : قد اشتهر تقسيم العلم إلى التصور والتصديق . واستبعده بعضهم لما بينهما من اللزوم إذ لا تصديق بدون التصور، بل ذكروا أنه لا تصور بحسب الحقيقة بدون التصديق بالتحقيق وإنما الكلام في التصور بحسب الاسم فعدلوا إلى التقسيم إلى التصور الساذج أي المشروط بعدم الحكم وإلى التصديق.
The second chapter: Knowledge, if it is a judgment, i.e., submission and acceptance of the relation, then it is assent; otherwise it is conception. Their difference is in reality, not merely in relation. I say: The division of knowledge into conception and assent has become famous. Some have rejected it because of the necessity between them, since there is no assent without conception; rather, they mentioned that there is no conception in reality without assent in actuality, but the discussion is about conception in name, so they modified to the division into bare conception, i.e., conditioned by absence of judgment, and assent.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This is a classification passage. The author states the famous division of knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). He notes an objection: they are inseparable because assent requires conception, and real conception involves some judgment (assent). To resolve, some scholars modify the division: conception is taken as bare conception (tasawwur sādhhij) without any judgment, and assent remains as acceptance of a relation. This clarifies that the division is by name and reality respectively.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
تصور ساذج
حكم
إذعان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conception and assent are completely independent categories.
Correction: The passage explains they are interdependent; assent requires conception, and real conception involves some judgment. The division is only valid when conception is taken as bare conception without judgment.

Checkpoint
What modification was made by some scholars to the division of knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq)?
A) They divided into tasawwur naqis and tasdiq.
B) They divided into tasawwur sādhhij (bare conception) and tasdiq.
C) They rejected the division entirely.
D) They added a third category.

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge into conception and assent is only valid when conception is understood as bare conception without any judgment.

What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
The Basic Division of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author states the basic division of knowledge into conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), and the key criterion of judgment (hukm) that distinguishes them.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either assent (judgment on a relation) or conception (apprehension without judgment).

This passage marks the beginning of our study of the division of knowledge. The author states a clear rule: if knowledge involves accepting or rejecting a relation, it is assent; otherwise it is conception. This distinction is the bedrock of logical and theological reasoning, as it defines how we process propositions and concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, including the parenthetical block and the first two footnotes. Notice the clear contrast: 'فتصديق وإلا فتصور'.
Pay attention to the term 'إذعاناً' in footnote (1).
2
Understand the criterion of judgment
The passage defines tasdiq as knowledge that involves a judgment (hukm) about a relation. This judgment is an acceptance (idhʿān) that the relation holds or does not hold. Contrast this with tasawwur, which lacks such judgment.
Tasawwur still involves apprehension but without affirming or denying the relation's actuality.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself: Why is this division important for Islamic theology? Because many theological arguments involve assenting to propositions about Allah and the world. Understanding this division clarifies the nature of evidence and belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
تقسيم العلم إلى تصديق وتصور
(المبحث الثاني : العلم إن كان حكماً أي إذعاناً (۱) وقبولاً للنسبة فتصديق وإلا فتصور (۲) واختلافهما بالحقيقة لا بمجرد الإضافة).
أقول : قد اشتهر تقسيم العلم إلى التصور (۳) والتصديق (٤) .
(۱) إذعاناً : بمعنى أن النفس أدركت أن النسبة واقعة في نفس الأمر أو ليست بواقعة، وتقرر ذلك عندها بأن لم تنفه جهلاً وهو معنى الإذعان والقبول.
(۲) وإذا لم يتعلق العلم بالنسبة الإيقاعية والانتزاعية بل تعلق بأحد طرفي النسبة أو بها لا على أنها واقعة أو ليست بواقعة في نفس الأمر بل على وجه اداركها مع احتمال الوقوع وعدمه فتصور.
(۳) التصور تصور الشيء تخيله وتصور له الشيء، صارت له عنده صورة وعند علماء النفس، هو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل . وعند المناطقة هو إدراك الماهية من غير أن يحكم عليها بنفي أو إثبات والفلاسفة يفرقون بين التصور القبلي والتصور البعدي فيقولون: إن التصور القبلي أو التصور المحض هو التصور المتقدم على التجربة كتصور الوحدة والكثرة وغيرهما، أما التصورات البعدية فهي المعاني المستمدة من التجربة، كتصور معنى الإنسان أو معنى الحيوان .
(٤) التصديق إما مركب أو بسيط فإن قلت إن التصديق هو علم إدراك الماهية مع الحكم عليها بالنفي أو الإثبات، كان التصديق مركباً مثال ذلك : أن تصديقك بأن العالم حادث مؤلف من تصور العالم وتصور ،الحدوث، ومن إدراك وقوع النسبة بينهما . وإذا قلت : إن التصديق هو مجرد إدراك النسبة كان التصديق بسيطاً. وهو على كل حال فعل عقلي يستلزم سبة الصدق إلى القائل وضده الإنكار والتكذيب.
The second chapter: Division of knowledge into assent and conception.
(The second chapter: knowledge, if it is a judgment, i.e., acceptance and admission of a relation, then it is assent; otherwise it is conception. Their difference is in reality, not merely by relation.)
I say: The division of knowledge into conception and assent has become well-known.
(1) Acceptance: meaning that the soul perceives that the relation is actually occurring or not occurring, and that is established for it such that it does not deny it out of ignorance, and that is the meaning of acceptance and admission.
(2) And if knowledge does not pertain to an existential or abstract relation, but rather pertains to one of the two terms of the relation or to the relation not as actually occurring or not occurring, but in the mode of grasping it with the possibility of occurrence and non-occurrence, then it is conception.
(3) Conception: imagining a thing, and the thing is conceived for him, a form becomes present with him. According to psychologists, it is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind. According to logicians, it is apprehension of the essence without judging it by negation or affirmation. The philosophers distinguish between a priori conception and a posteriori conception, saying that a priori or pure conception is the conception that precedes experience, like the conception of unity and multiplicity etc., while a posteriori conceptions are meanings derived from experience, like the conception of the meaning of human or the meaning of animal.
(4) Assent: either composite or simple. If you say that assent is the knowledge of apprehending the essence with judgment upon it by negation or affirmation, then assent is composite. Example: your assent that the world is created is composed of the conception of the world, the conception of creation, and the apprehension of the occurrence of the relation between them. If you say that assent is mere apprehension of the relation, then assent is simple. In any case, it is a mental act that entails attributing truth to the speaker, and its opposite is denial and falsification.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage defines the foundational division of knowledge (al-ilm) into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). It begins with a parenthetical definition: if knowledge involves a judgment (hukm) about a relation — accepting it as true or false — it is tasdiq; otherwise, it is tasawwur. The author notes that this division is well-known. The footnotes provide extensive clarification. Footnote (1) defines the 'acceptance' (idhʿān) in tasdiq as the soul's perception that a relation actually holds or does not hold, with no denial due to ignorance. Footnote (2) specifies that if knowledge does not involve judgment about a relation but merely apprehends a term or the relation without affirming or denying its actuality, it is tasawwur. Footnote (3) gives multiple definitions of tasawwur: psychologists see it as the form of a thing in the mind; logicians as apprehension of essence without judgment; philosophers distinguish a priori (e.g., unity, multiplicity) from a posteriori (e.g., 'human') conceptions. Footnote (4) divides tasdiq into composite (e.g., 'the world is created' involves conceiving subject, predicate, and relation) and simple (mere apprehension of the relation). It ends by noting that tasdiq is a mental act requiring attributing truth (sidq) to the speaker, as opposed to denial (inkār) and falsification (takdhīb). The passage thus establishes the precise meanings of these two categories, which are essential for kalam reasoning.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
حكم
إذعان
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That tasawwur and tasdiq are independent categories without any connection.
Correction: While they are distinct, tasdiq always depends on tasawwur, because one must first conceive the terms before judging the relation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of tasdiq?
A) It is conception without judgment.
B) It involves a judgment about a relation.
C) It is the same as tasawwur.
D) It is only about universals.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is divided into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent); the latter involves a judgment on a relation while the former does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge of Allah: is it merely a conception (tasawwur) of His names and attributes, or has it become an assent (tasdiq) that settles in your heart with certainty? Today, pick one divine attribute you have only conceived and strive to know it as a reality that demands your full acceptance.
?
When you recite the Quran, do you approach it as a collection of concepts or as a source of judgments that require your personal acceptance? Identify one verse you have understood only as a concept and commit to receiving it now as an assent that changes how you live.
?
The author says the difference between conception and assent is real, not relative. In your spiritual life, are you content with just conceiving truths about faith, or are you actively seeking to affirm them? Write down one Islamic truth you have only conceived and make a concrete plan to interiorize it as an assent through study, reflection, and action.

Detailed Definitions of Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author elaborates on tasawwur and tasdiq with specific definitions and subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple definitions of tasawwur (from psychology, logic, philosophy) and the classification of tasdiq into composite and simple.

Core Idea
Tasawwur is apprehension without judgment; tasdiq is judgment on a relation, either composite or simple.

Having established the basic division, the passage now provides nuanced definitions of tasawwur from three perspectives and explains the two types of tasdiq. This deepens our understanding of how knowledge is structured in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of tasawwur
Read footnote (3) carefully. It gives three definitions: from common usage, psychologists, and logicians. Also note the distinction between a priori and a posteriori conceptions.
Notice how each definition emphasizes that tasawwur lacks judgment.
2
Understand the two types of tasdiq
Read footnote (4). Tasdiq is either composite (complex, involving multiple parts) or simple (mere apprehension of relation). The example 'the world is created' illustrates the composite type.
The composite view sees tasdiq as composed of three elements.
3
Reflect on the mental act of tasdiq
The passage concludes that tasdiq, whether composite or simple, is a mental act that entails attributing truth to the speaker (sidq). Its opposite is denial (inkār) and falsification (takdhīb).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the Oneness of Allah (tawhid), do you merely conceive of it as an abstract idea, or have you genuinely assented to it with your heart and judged it to be true? Which of the two (tasawwur or tasdiq) does your iman currently rely on?
?
The passage says that assent always involves attributing truth to the speaker. When you hear a Qur'anic verse or a hadith, do you instantly assent to it, or do you find yourself hesitating, denying, or falsifying? Where does your soul stand on the truth of Revelation right now?
?
You have just learned that conception can be a priori (innate) or a posteriori (learned). Which of your beliefs about Allah came purely from your innate nature (fitrah), and which came from hearing or learning? Are you using your fitrah correctly, or have you covered it with doubts and arguments?

The Objection of Reflective Knowledge and Its Resolution
Objection and refutation regarding the definition of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between perpetual and temporary inability resolves the apparent contradiction between reflective knowledge and necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
After acquiring theoretical knowledge, you cannot unlearn it, but you could have chosen not to learn it.

When we say necessary knowledge is knowledge we cannot separate from, a challenge arises: what about knowledge gained by reflection? Once you understand something, you cannot unthink it. The scholars answer by clarifying the timing of inability—perpetual versus after acquisition. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection: knowledge by reflection seems to contradict the definition of necessary knowledge because once obtained, one cannot separate from it.
Notice that the objector is using the same criterion (inability to separate) to claim that reflective knowledge is necessary.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
Read the answer carefully. It distinguishes between necessary knowledge (where inability is perpetual) and theoretical knowledge (where inability only occurs after acquisition).
The key phrase is 'نفي القدرة دائماً' vs 'تنتفي القدرة بعد الحصول'.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider an example: knowing that 2+2=4. You cannot unlearn it, but before learning it you could have chosen not to engage in the reasoning. This shows it is theoretical, not necessary.
This solidifies why reflective knowledge does not fall under the definition of necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يرد على طرد العبارتين العلم الحاصل بالنظر، إذ لا قدرة حينئذ على تحصيله ولا على الانفكاك عنه . أجيب بأن المعتبر في الضروري نفي القدرة دائماً، وفي النظري إنما تنتفي القدرة بعد الحصول إذ قبله يقتدر على التحصيل بأن يكتسب وعلى الانفكاك بأن لا يكتسب
If it is said: The objection against the generality of the two phrases is the knowledge obtained by reflection, for there is no power at that time to acquire it nor to separate from it. It is answered: That what is considered in the necessary (knowledge) is the negation of power perpetually, and in the theoretical (knowledge) power is only negated after obtaining, because before it one has the power to acquire by earning and to separate by not earning.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of necessary (ضروري) and theoretical (نظري) knowledge. The objector claims that knowledge obtained by reflection (العلم الحاصل بالنظر) contradicts the definition because once obtained, the knower cannot separate from it, making it seem necessary. The answer clarifies the distinction: in necessary knowledge, the inability to separate is perpetual; in theoretical knowledge, the inability only occurs after acquisition. Before acquisition, one has the power to either acquire or not acquire. Thus the objection is resolved.

Key Terms
ضروري
نظري
نظر
قدرة
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if you cannot separate from acquired knowledge, it must be necessary knowledge.
Correction: Necessary knowledge is defined by perpetual inability to separate, whereas theoretical knowledge only has inability after acquisition; before acquisition, you had the power to choose.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what distinguishes necessary knowledge from theoretical knowledge?
A) Necessary knowledge cannot be acquired, while theoretical can.
B) In necessary knowledge, inability to separate is perpetual; in theoretical, it is only after acquisition.
C) Necessary knowledge is known by all, theoretical only by scholars.
D) Theoretical knowledge does not involve power at all.

Key Takeaway
The defining difference between necessary and theoretical knowledge is not the ability to separate after acquisition, but the presence of choice before acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a truth about Allah or your faith that you once had the power to learn but chose to ignore? What would change in your life if you stopped avoiding it?
?
Once you have truly understood a command of Allah, can you ever un-know it? How does that knowledge weigh on your choices today?
?
Before you acquire deeper knowledge of your religion, you have the power to walk away. Are you using that power to stay comfortable in ignorance, or are you earnestly seeking? What will you commit to learning starting today?

Acquired, Necessary, and Theoretical Knowledge
Classification of knowledge into acquired (كسبي) and necessary (ضروري), and synonyms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different definitions of acquired knowledge and how it relates to necessary and theoretical knowledge in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is not always synonymous with theoretical; its definition depends on the method of acquisition.

This passage from Sanusi's work clarifies the terminology of knowledge classification. It explains that the term 'acquired' (كسبي) can have different shades of meaning depending on the theological framework. By understanding these nuances, you will better grasp the foundation of epistemic categories in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the two perspectives on acquisition.
2
Identify the two perspectives
The passage mentions two views: one where acquisition is only through reflection, and another where it includes voluntary engagement with causes like intellect or senses.
These correspond to different theological schools.
3
Understand the synonym relationship
In the first view, acquired is synonymous with theoretical. In the second, acquired is broader. Note the term 'inferential' (استدلالي) as a specification of theoretical.
4
Apply the concept
Think of examples of acquired knowledge through reflection (like logical deduction) and through direct causes (like sensory perception).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكسبي يقابل الضروري ويرادف النظري فيمن يجعل طريق الاكتساب هو النظر لا غير . وأما فيمن جوز الكسب بمثل التصفية والإلهام من خوارق العادات وقد يقال الكسبي لما يحصل بمباشرة الأسباب اختياراً كصرف العقل أو الحس. والضروري لما يقابله ويخص الكسبي النظري باسم النظري باسم الاستدلالي.
And the acquired (الكسبي) contrasts with the necessary (الضروري) and is synonymous with the theoretical (النظري) in the view of one who makes the way of acquisition reflection only. But as for one who permits acquisition by means such as purification and inspiration from supernatural habits, the acquired is said to be what is obtained by directly engaging the causes voluntarily, such as directing the intellect or sense. And the necessary is what contrasts with it. And the theoretical (النظري) is specified with the name 'inferential' (الاستدلالي).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the terminology. It states that the acquired (الكسبي) is the opposite of the necessary (الضروري) and is synonymous with theoretical (النظري) for those who hold that acquisition is only through reflection. However, for those who allow acquisition through supernatural means like purification and inspiration, the acquired is defined as what is obtained by directly engaging causes voluntarily, such as using the intellect or senses. The necessary is its counterpart. Additionally, the theoretical is specifically called 'inferential' (الاستدلالي). This clarifies the range of meanings of these key terms in kalam.

Key Terms
كسبي
ضروري
نظري
استدلالي
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that acquired knowledge (كسبي) and theoretical knowledge (نظري) are always identical.
Correction: The passage shows that they are synonymous only in one view; in another, acquired can be broader, including knowledge gained through voluntary causes like sensory perception.

Checkpoint
How is the 'acquired' (الكسبي) defined according to those who permit acquisition through non-reflective means?
A) It is synonymous with theoretical.
B) It is obtained by directly engaging causes voluntarily.
C) It is the same as necessary.
D) It is only through reflection.

Key Takeaway
The term 'acquired knowledge' has a broader definition that includes voluntary engagement with causes, not merely reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek certainty about your faith, do you trust only your own reasoning or have you ever pursued spiritual purification as a method of knowing? Which one do you rely on more, and why?
?
This lesson distinguishes between necessary and acquired knowledge. Is your belief in Allah a necessary knowledge for you, or have you merely acquired it from others? What would it take for it to become truly necessary?

What Is Kalam?
